        <p id="mwGQ"><b id="mwGg">The Monkees</b> were an American <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pop_rock" title="Pop rock" id="mwGw">pop rock</a> band formed in Los Angeles in the mid-1960s.<!-- Sources differ as to whether the band was formed in 1965 or in 1966. --> The band consisted of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Micky_Dolenz" title="Micky Dolenz" id="mwHA">Micky Dolenz</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Davy_Jones_(musician)" title="Davy Jones (musician)" id="mwHQ">Davy Jones</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Michael_Nesmith" title="Michael Nesmith" id="mwHg">Michael Nesmith</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Peter_Tork" title="Peter Tork" id="mwHw">Peter Tork</a>. Spurred by the success of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Monkees_(TV_series)" title="The Monkees (TV series)" id="mwIA">the television show of the same name</a>, the Monkees were one of the most successful bands of the late 1960s. With international hits, four chart-topping albums and three chart-topping songs ("<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Last_Train_to_Clarksville" title="Last Train to Clarksville" id="mwIQ">Last Train to Clarksville</a>", "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./I'm_a_Believer" title="I'm a Believer" id="mwIg">I'm a Believer</a>", and "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Daydream_Believer" title="Daydream Believer" id="mwIw">Daydream Believer</a>"), they sold more than 75 million records worldwide.</p>
        
        <p id="mwJA">The Monkees were originally a fictional band created for the NBC television sitcom of the same name. Dolenz, Jones, Nesmith and Tork were cast to portray members of a band in the sitcom. Music credited to the Monkees appeared in the sitcom and was released on LPs and singles beginning in 1966, and the sitcom aired from 1966 to 1968. At first, the band members' musical contributions were primarily limited to lead vocals and the occasional composition, with the remaining music provided by professional songwriters and studio musicians. Though this arrangement yielded multiple hit albums and singles, the band members revolted and, after a brief power struggle, gained full control over the recording process in 1967. For two albums, the Monkees mostly performed as a group; however, within a year, each member was pursuing his own interests under the Monkees' name, rendering the Monkees once again a group in name only. With widespread allegations that the band members did not play their own instruments—followed by the cancellation of <i id="mwJQ">The Monkees</i>, diminishing success on the charts, and waning popularity overall—band members began to leave the group. The Monkees held a final recording session in 1970 before breaking up.</p><!-- Sources differ as to whether the band disbanded in 1970 or 1971. -->
        
        <p id="mwJg">A revival of interest in the Monkees came in 1986, prompting a 20th anniversary reunion. Over the following 35 years, the Monkees periodically reunited for reunion tours, a major-network television special, and new studio albums. Following Jones's death in 2012 and Tork's death in 2019, Dolenz and Nesmith embarked on a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Farewell_tour" title="Farewell tour" class="mw-redirect" id="mwJw">farewell tour</a> in 2021. The tour ended shortly before Nesmith's death at the end of the year.</p>
        
        <meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" data-mw='{"autoGenerated":true}' id="mwKA"/></section><section data-mw-section-id="1" id="mwKQ"><h2 id="History">History</h2>
        <section data-mw-section-id="2" id="mwKg"><h3 id="Conception_and_casting">Conception and casting</h3>
        <p id="mwKw">The Monkees were formed in the mid-1960s in Los Angeles.<sup about="#mwt55" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-6" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-6"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-6" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 5;" id="mwLA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwLQ">[5]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwLg">Aspiring filmmaker <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bob_Rafelson" title="Bob Rafelson" id="mwLw">Bob Rafelson</a> developed the initial idea for <i id="mwMA">The Monkees</i> in 1962 and tried selling it to Revue, the television division of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures" id="mwMQ">Universal Pictures</a>, but was unsuccessful.<sup about="#mwt59" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage15_7-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage15"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage15-7"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage15-7" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 6;" id="mwMg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwMw">[6]</span></a></sup> In May 1964, while working at <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Screen_Gems" title="Screen Gems" id="mwNA">Screen Gems</a>, Rafelson teamed up with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bert_Schneider" title="Bert Schneider" id="mwNQ">Bert Schneider</a>, whose father, Abraham Schneider, headed the Colpix Records and Screen Gems Television units of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures" id="mwNg">Columbia Pictures</a>. Rafelson and Schneider ultimately formed Raybert Productions.<sup about="#mwt60" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage18_8-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage18"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage18-8"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage18-8" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 7;" id="mwNw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwOA">[7]</span></a></sup> The Beatles' films <i id="mwOQ"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./A_Hard_Day's_Night_(film)" title="A Hard Day's Night (film)" id="mwOg">A Hard Day's Night</a></i> and <i id="mwOw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Help!_(film)" title="Help! (film)" id="mwPA">Help!</a></i> inspired Rafelson and Schneider to revive Rafelson's idea for <i id="mwPQ">The Monkees</i>. As "Raybert Productions", they sold the show to Screen Gems Television on April 16, 1965.<sup about="#mwt64" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-9" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-9"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-9" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 8;" id="mwPg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwPw">[8]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwQA">Rafelson and Schneider's original idea was to cast an existing New York folk rock group, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Lovin'_Spoonful" title="The Lovin' Spoonful" id="mwQQ">the Lovin' Spoonful</a>, who were not widely known at the time. After those plans fell through, Rafelson and Schneider focused on Davy Jones. In September 1964, Jones had signed to a long-term contract to appear in TV programs for Screen Gems, to make feature films for Columbia Pictures and to record music for the Colpix label.<sup about="#mwt68" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage19_10-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage19"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage19-10"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage19-10" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 9;" id="mwQg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwQw">[9]</span></a></sup> His involvement with <i id="mwRA">The Monkees</i> was publicly announced on July 14, 1965.<sup about="#mwt69" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage24_11-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage24"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage24-11"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage24-11" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 10;" id="mwRQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwRg">[10]</span></a></sup> Jones had previously starred as the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Artful_Dodger" title="Artful Dodger" id="mwRw">Artful Dodger</a> in the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Broadway_theater" title="Broadway theater" class="mw-redirect" id="mwSA">Broadway theater</a> show <i id="mwSQ"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Oliver!" title="Oliver!" id="mwSg">Oliver!</a></i>; for his work in <i id="mwSw">Oliver!</i>, he was nominated for a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tony_Award_for_Best_Featured_Actor_in_a_Musical" title="Tony Award for Best Featured Actor in a Musical" id="mwTA">Tony Award for Best Featured Actor in a Musical</a> in 1963.<sup about="#mwt70" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage16_12-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage16"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage16-12"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage16-12" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 11;" id="mwTQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwTg">[11]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwTw">In September<span class="nowrap" about="#mwt73" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"nbsp","href":"./Template:Nbsp"},"params":{},"i":0}}]}' id="mwUA"><span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span></span>1965, <i id="mwUQ"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Daily_Variety" title="Daily Variety" class="mw-redirect" id="mwUg">Daily Variety</a></i><sup about="#mwt77" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_13-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto1"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-auto1-13"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-auto1-13" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 12;" id="mwUw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwVA">[12]</span></a></sup> and <i id="mwVQ">The Hollywood Reporter</i><sup about="#mwt81" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto_14-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-auto-14"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-auto-14" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 13;" id="mwVg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwVw">[13]</span></a></sup> ran advertisements to cast the remainder of the band/cast members for the TV show. The advertisements each read as follows:</p>
        
        <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1211633275" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" about="#mwt84" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"blockquote","href":"./Template:Blockquote"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Madness!! Auditions. Folk &amp; Roll Musicians-Singers for acting roles in new TV series. Running parts for 4 insane boys, age 17–21. Want spirited [[Ben Frank&apos;s]]-types. Have courage to work. Must come down for interview.&lt;ref name=\"auto1\"/>&lt;ref name=\"auto\"/>"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwWA">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote " about="#mwt84" id="mwWQ"><p>Madness!! Auditions. Folk &amp; Roll Musicians-Singers for acting roles in new TV series. Running parts for 4 insane boys, age 17–21. Want spirited <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ben_Frank's" title="Ben Frank's">Ben Frank's</a>-types. Have courage to work. Must come down for interview.<sup about="#mwt89" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_13-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto1"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-auto1-13" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 12;" id="mwWg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwWw">[12]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt90" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-auto_14-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"auto"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-auto-14" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 13;" id="mwXA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwXQ">[13]</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
        
        <p id="mwXg">Out of 437 applicants,<sup about="#mwt95" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Sandoval_15-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Sandoval"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Sandoval-15"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Sandoval-15" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 14;" id="mwXw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwYA">[14]</span></a></sup> the other three chosen for the cast of the TV show were musician Michael Nesmith, musician Peter Tork, and actor/musician Micky Dolenz. Nesmith had been working as a musician since early 1963 and had been recording and releasing music under various names, including Michael Blessing and "Mike &amp; John &amp; Bill", and he had studied drama in college. Of the final three, Nesmith was the only one who actually saw the ad in <i id="mwYQ">Daily Variety</i> and <i id="mwYg">The Hollywood Reporter</i>. Tork, the last to be chosen, had been working the Greenwich Village scene as a musician and had shared the stage with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pete_Seeger" title="Pete Seeger" id="mwYw">Pete Seeger</a>; he learned of <i id="mwZA">The Monkees</i> from <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Stephen_Stills" title="Stephen Stills" id="mwZQ">Stephen Stills</a>, whom Rafelson and Schneider had rejected as a songwriter. Dolenz was an actor (his father was veteran character actor <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./George_Dolenz" title="George Dolenz" id="mwZg">George Dolenz</a>) who had starred in the Screen Gems-produced TV series <i id="mwZw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Circus_Boy" title="Circus Boy" id="mwaA">Circus Boy</a></i> as a child, using the stage name Mickey Braddock. He had also played guitar and sung in a band called the Missing Links, which released one single, "Don't Do It".</p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="3" id="mwaQ"><h3 id="Early_years">Early years</h3>
        
        <section data-mw-section-id="4" id="mwag"><h4 id="Developing_the_music_for_their_debut_album">Developing the music for their debut album</h4>
        <figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwaw"><a href="./File:The_Monkees.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwbA"><img resource="./File:The_Monkees.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/The_Monkees.jpg/220px-The_Monkees.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1364" data-file-height="977" data-file-type="bitmap" height="158" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/The_Monkees.jpg/330px-The_Monkees.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/The_Monkees.jpg/440px-The_Monkees.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element" id="mwbQ"/></a><figcaption id="mwbg">The Monkees' chairs</figcaption></figure>
        
        <p id="mwbw">During the casting process, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Don_Kirshner" title="Don Kirshner" id="mwcA">Don Kirshner</a>, Screen Gems' head of music, was contacted to secure music for <i id="mwcQ">The Monkees</i> pilot. Kirshner's <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Brill_Building" title="Brill Building" id="mwcg">Brill Building</a> firm <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Aldon_Music" title="Aldon Music" id="mwcw">Aldon Music</a> had an extensive portfolio of songwriters, many in need of work after the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./British_Invasion" title="British Invasion" id="mwdA">British Invasion</a> had reorganized the American music scene; while several Aldon writers contributed songs to the Monkees during their existence, the bulk of the songwriting for the group fell upon Tommy Boyce and Bobby Hart, two songwriters who were only beginning to break through to success at the time.<sup about="#mwt99" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage27_16-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage27"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage27-16"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage27-16" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 15;" id="mwdQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwdg">[15]</span></a></sup> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Boyce_and_Hart" title="Boyce and Hart" id="mwdw">Boyce and Hart</a> contributed four demo recordings for the pilot.<sup about="#mwt100" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage40_17-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage40"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage40-17"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage40-17" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 16;" id="mweA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mweQ">[16]</span></a></sup> One of these recordings was "(Theme From) The Monkees" which helped get the series the green light.<sup about="#mwt101" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-18" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-18"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-18" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 17;" id="mweg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwew">[17]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwfA">When <i id="mwfQ">The Monkees</i> was picked up as a series, development of the musical side of the project accelerated. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures" id="mwfg">Columbia</a>-<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Screen_Gems" title="Screen Gems" id="mwfw">Screen Gems</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./RCA_Victor" title="RCA Victor" class="mw-redirect" id="mwgA">RCA Victor</a> entered into a joint venture called <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Colgems_Records" title="Colgems Records" id="mwgQ">Colgems Records</a>; the primary purpose of the venture was to distribute Monkees records.<sup about="#mwt105" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage36_19-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage36"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage36-19"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage36-19" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 18;" id="mwgg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwgw">[18]</span></a></sup> Raybert set up a rehearsal space and rented instruments for the group to practice playing in April 1966,<sup about="#mwt106" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage84_20-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage84"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage84-20"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage84-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mwhA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwhQ">[19]</span></a></sup> but it quickly became apparent they would not be in shape in time for the series debut. The producers called upon Kirshner to recruit a producer for the Monkees' sessions.<sup about="#mwt107" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage37_21-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage37"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage37-21"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage37-21" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 20;" id="mwhg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwhw">[20]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwiA">Kirshner called on <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Snuff_Garrett" title="Snuff Garrett" id="mwiQ">Snuff Garrett</a>, composer of several hits by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Gary_Lewis_&amp;_the_Playboys" title="Gary Lewis &amp; the Playboys" id="mwig">Gary Lewis &amp; the Playboys</a>, to produce the initial musical cuts for the show. Garrett, upon meeting the four Monkees in June 1966, decided that Jones would sing lead, a choice that was unpopular with the group. This cool reception led Kirshner to drop Garrett and buy out his contract.<sup about="#mwt112" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage39_22-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage39"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage39-22"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage39-22" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 21;" id="mwiw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwjA">[21]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwjQ">Kirshner next allowed Nesmith to produce sessions, provided he did not play on any tracks he produced.<sup about="#mwt117" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage84_20-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage84"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage84-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mwjg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwjw">[19]</span></a></sup> Nesmith did, however, start using the other Monkees on his sessions, particularly Tork as a guitarist.<sup about="#mwt118" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-23" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-23"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-23" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 22;" id="mwkA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwkQ">[22]</span></a></sup> Kirshner came back to the enthusiastic Boyce and Hart to be the regular producers, but he brought in one of his top East Coast associates, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jack_Keller_(songwriter)" title="Jack Keller (songwriter)" id="mwkg">Jack Keller</a>, to lend some production experience to the sessions.<sup about="#mwt119" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage84_20-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage84"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage84-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mwkw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwlA">[19]</span></a></sup> Boyce and Hart quickly realized that, when together, the four actors fooled around and tried to crack each other up. Because of this, the producers often brought in each singer individually.<sup about="#mwt120" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage46_24-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage46"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage46-24"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage46-24" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 23;" id="mwlQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwlg">[23]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwlw">The Monkees' debut and second albums were meant to be a soundtrack to the first season of the TV show, to cash in on the audience. In the 2006 Rhino Deluxe Edition re-issue of their second album, <i id="mwmA">More of the Monkees</i>, Nesmith stated that he was angered by the release of the first album because it portrayed the band as an actual rock-and-roll band and gave no credit to the other musicians involved in the project.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_unsourced_statements" about="#mwt121" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"citation needed","href":"./Template:Citation_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"October 2023"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwmQ"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_unsourced_statements_from_October_2023" about="#mwt121"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt121" id="mwmg"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwmw">The Monkees' first single, "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Last_Train_to_Clarksville" title="Last Train to Clarksville" id="mwnA">Last Train to Clarksville</a>" b/w "Take a Giant Step", was released in August 1966, just weeks prior to the TV broadcast debut. In conjunction with the first broadcast of the television show on September 12, 1966, on the NBC television network, NBC and Columbia had a major hit on their hands.<sup about="#mwt124" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_44,track_2_25-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"sfn","href":"./Template:Sfn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"Gilliland"},"2":{"wt":"1969"},"loc":{"wt":"show 44,track 2"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGilliland1969show_44,track_2-25" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 24;" id="mwnQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwng">[24]</span></a></sup> The single topped the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100" id="mwnw"><i id="mwoA">Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> for the week ending November 5, 1966.<sup about="#mwt130" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-26" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-26"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-26" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 25;" id="mwoQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwog">[25]</span></a></sup> The Monkees' debut album, <i id="mwow"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Monkees_(album)" title="The Monkees (album)" id="mwpA">The Monkees</a></i>, was released a month later; it spent 13 weeks at No. 1 and stayed on the Billboard charts for 78 weeks. Twenty years later, during their reunion, it spent another 24 weeks on the Billboard charts.<sup about="#mwt134" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-27" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-27"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-27" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 26;" id="mwpQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwpg">[26]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="5" id="mwpw"><h4 id="Lineup_configuration">Lineup configuration</h4>
        <figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwqA"><a href="./File:Davy_Jones_Peter_Tork_The_Monkees_1966.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwqQ"><img resource="./File:Davy_Jones_Peter_Tork_The_Monkees_1966.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Davy_Jones_Peter_Tork_The_Monkees_1966.jpg/220px-Davy_Jones_Peter_Tork_The_Monkees_1966.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="520" data-file-height="650" data-file-type="bitmap" height="275" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Davy_Jones_Peter_Tork_The_Monkees_1966.jpg/330px-Davy_Jones_Peter_Tork_The_Monkees_1966.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Davy_Jones_Peter_Tork_The_Monkees_1966.jpg/440px-Davy_Jones_Peter_Tork_The_Monkees_1966.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element" id="mwqg"/></a><figcaption id="mwqw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Davy_Jones_(musician)" title="Davy Jones (musician)" id="mwrA">Davy Jones</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Peter_Tork" title="Peter Tork" id="mwrQ">Peter Tork</a> in 1966</figcaption></figure>
        <p id="mwrg">In assigning instruments for purposes of the television show, a dilemma arose as to which of the four would portray the drummer. Both Nesmith (a skilled guitarist and bassist) and Tork (who could play several stringed and keyboard instruments) were peripherally familiar with the instrument, but both declined to give the drum set a try. Jones knew how to play the drums and tested well enough on the instrument; however, the producers believed that he would be virtually hidden from view behind a drum set due to his short stature. Thus, Dolenz (who knew only how to play the guitar) was assigned to become the drummer. Tork taught Dolenz his first few beats on the drums, enough for him to fake his way through filming the pilot. He was soon taught how to play properly.<sup about="#mwt141" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-28" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-28"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-28" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 27;" id="mwrw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwsA">[27]</span></a></sup> The lineup for the TV show most frequently featured Nesmith on guitar, Tork on bass, Dolenz on drums and Jones as a frontman, singer and percussionist, despite the fact that this lineup did not correspond to the members' musical strengths. Tork was a more experienced guitar player than Nesmith, while Nesmith had trained on the bass. Also, while Jones had a strong lead voice and did sing lead on several Monkees recordings, Dolenz's voice is regarded, particularly by Nesmith, as distinctive and a hallmark of the Monkees' sound.<sup about="#mwt145" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-29" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-29"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-29" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 28;" id="mwsQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwsg">[28]</span></a></sup><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_lacking_reliable_references" about="#mwt137" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"better source needed","href":"./Template:Better_source_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"October 2023"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwsw"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_lacking_reliable_references_from_October_2023" about="#mwt137"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt137" id="mwtA"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (October 2023)">better<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>source<span typeof="mw:Entity"> </span>needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwtQ">Unlike most television shows of the time, <i id="mwtg">The Monkees</i> episodes were written with many setups, requiring frequent breaks to prepare the set and cameras for short bursts of filming. Some of the "bursts" are considered proto-music videos, inasmuch as they were produced to sell the records. <i id="mwtw">The Monkees Tale</i> author Eric Lefcowitz noted that the Monkees were—first and foremost—a video group.<sup about="#mwt150" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Lefcowitz_30-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Lefcowitz"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Lefcowitz-30"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Lefcowitz-30" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 29;" id="mwuA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwuQ">[29]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="6" id="mwug"><h4 id="Live_performances_and_touring">Live performances and touring</h4>
        <p id="mwuw">Pleased with their initial efforts, Columbia (over Kirshner's objections) planned to send the Monkees out to play <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Concert" title="Concert" id="mwvA">live concerts</a>. The massive success of the series—and its spin-off records—created intense pressure to mount a touring version of the group. Against the initial wishes of the producers, the band went out on the road and made their debut live performance in December 1966 in Hawaii.<sup about="#mwt155" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-31" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-31"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-31" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 30;" id="mwvQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwvg">[30]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwvw">The results of these live performances were far better than expected. Wherever they went, the group was greeted by scenes of fan adulation reminiscent of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Beatlemania" title="Beatlemania" id="mwwA">Beatlemania</a>. This gave the singers increased confidence in their fight for control over the musical material chosen for the series.<sup about="#mwt160" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-baker_32-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"baker"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-baker-32"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-baker-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwwQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwwg">[31]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section></section><section data-mw-section-id="7" id="mwww"><h3 id="Independence">Independence</h3>
        <figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwxA"><a href="./File:The_Monkees_1967.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwxQ"><img resource="./File:The_Monkees_1967.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/The_Monkees_1967.jpg/220px-The_Monkees_1967.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="965" data-file-height="732" data-file-type="bitmap" height="167" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/The_Monkees_1967.jpg/330px-The_Monkees_1967.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/The_Monkees_1967.jpg/440px-The_Monkees_1967.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element" id="mwxg"/></a><figcaption id="mwxw">The Monkees in 1965</figcaption></figure>
        
        <section data-mw-section-id="8" id="mwyA"><h4 id="Conflict_with_Kirshner">Conflict with Kirshner</h4>
        <p id="mwyQ">In early 1967, controversy concerning the Monkees' studio abilities arose. Dolenz told a reporter that <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Wrecking_Crew_(music)" title="The Wrecking Crew (music)" id="mwyg">the Wrecking Crew</a> provided the backing tracks for the first two Monkees albums, and that his position as drummer was simply because a Monkee had to learn to play the drums, and he only knew the guitar.<sup about="#mwt166" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-dolenz_33-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"dolenz"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-dolenz-33"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-dolenz-33" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 32;" id="mwyw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwzA">[32]</span></a></sup> In the January 28, 1967, issue of <i id="mwzQ">Saturday Evening Post</i> an article quoted Nesmith railing against the music creation process. "Do you know how debilitating it is to sit up and have to duplicate somebody else's records?" he asked. "Tell the world we don't record our own music."<sup about="#mwt167" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-34" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-34"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-34" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 33;" id="mwzg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwzw">[33]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mw0A">The band members were displeased that the music publishing company would not allow them to play their own instruments on their records or to use more of their own material. These complaints intensified when Kirshner moved track recording from California to New York, leaving the band out of the musical process entirely until they were called upon to add their vocals to the completed tracks. Nesmith, when asked about the situation by <i id="mw0Q">Rolling Stone</i> magazine, said, "The [TV show's] producers [in Hollywood] backed us and David went along. None of us could have fought the battles we did [with the music publishers] without the explicit support of the show's producers".<sup about="#mwt172" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-35" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-35"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-35" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 34;" id="mw0g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw0w">[34]</span></a></sup></p> 
        <figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mw1A"><a href="./File:The_Monkees_March_1967.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mw1Q"><img resource="./File:The_Monkees_March_1967.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/The_Monkees_March_1967.jpg/220px-The_Monkees_March_1967.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="735" data-file-height="705" data-file-type="bitmap" height="211" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/The_Monkees_March_1967.jpg/330px-The_Monkees_March_1967.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/The_Monkees_March_1967.jpg/440px-The_Monkees_March_1967.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element" id="mw1g"/></a><figcaption id="mw1w">Publicity shot in 1967</figcaption></figure>
        
        <p id="mw2A">On January 16, 1967, the Monkees held their first recording session as a fully functioning, self-contained band. The band recorded an early version of Nesmith's self-composed top 40 hit single "The Girl I Knew Somewhere", along with "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./All_of_Your_Toys" title="All of Your Toys" id="mw2Q">All of Your Toys</a>" and "She's So Far Out, She's In".<sup about="#mwt175" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage82_36-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage82"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage82-36"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage82-36" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 35;" id="mw2g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw2w">[35]</span></a></sup> Also in January, Kirshner released the band's second album of songs that used session musicians, <i id="mw3A">More of the Monkees</i>, without the band's knowledge. The Monkees were annoyed at not having even been told of the release in advance, at having their opinions on the track selection ignored, at Kirshner's self-congratulatory liner notes and also because of the amateurish-looking cover art, which was merely a composite of pictures of the four taken for a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./J.C._Penney" title="J.C. Penney" class="mw-redirect" id="mw3Q">J.C. Penney</a> clothing advertisement. Indeed, the Monkees had not even been given a copy of the album; they had to buy it from a record store.<sup about="#mwt176" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage80_37-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage80"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage80-37"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage80-37" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 36;" id="mw3g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw3w">[36]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mw4A">The climax of the rivalry between Kirshner and the band was an intense argument among Nesmith, Kirshner and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Colgems" title="Colgems" class="mw-redirect" id="mw4Q">Colgems</a> lawyer Herb Moelis, which took place at the Beverly Hills Hotel in January 1967. Kirshner had presented the group with royalty checks and gold records. Nesmith had responded with an ultimatum, demanding a change in the way the Monkees' music was chosen and recorded. Moelis reminded Nesmith that he was under contract. The confrontation ended with Nesmith punching a hole in a wall and saying, "That could have been your face!" However, each of the members, including Nesmith, accepted the $250,000 royalty checks (equivalent to approximately $<span about="#mwt178" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"formatnum:{{Inflation|US|250000|1967|r=-5}}","function":"formatnum"},"params":{},"i":0}}]}' id="mw4g">2,300,000</span> in today's funds).<sup about="#mwt180" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_38-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Transclusion mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"inflation-fn","href":"./Template:Inflation-fn"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"US"}},"i":0}}]}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-inflation-US-38" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 37;" id="mw4w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw5A">[37]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt189" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage80_37-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage80"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage80-37" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 36;" id="mw5Q"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw5g">[36]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mw5w">Soon after, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Colgems" title="Colgems" class="mw-redirect" id="mw6A">Colgems</a> and the Monkees reached an agreement not to release material directly created by the group together with unrelated Kirshner-produced material. Kirshner immediately violated this agreement in early February 1967, when he released "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./A_Little_Bit_Me,_A_Little_Bit_You" title="A Little Bit Me, A Little Bit You" class="mw-redirect" id="mw6Q">A Little Bit Me, A Little Bit You</a>", composed and written by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neil_Diamond" title="Neil Diamond" id="mw6g">Neil Diamond</a>, as a single with an early version of "She Hangs Out", a song recorded in New York with Davy Jones's vocals, as the B-side. (This single was only released in Canada and was withdrawn after a couple of weeks.<sup about="#mwt195" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Discography_39-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Discography"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Discography-39"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Discography-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mw6w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw7A">[38]</span></a></sup>) He was consequently dismissed from the project.<sup about="#mwt199" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-40" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-40"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-40" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 39;" id="mw7Q"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw7g">[39]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mw7w">Propelled by the band's second single, "I'm a Believer" b/w "(I'm Not Your) Steppin' Stone", <i id="mw8A">More of the Monkees</i> became the band's biggest-selling LP. The album spent 70 weeks on the Billboard charts, staying No. 1 for 18 weeks<sup about="#mwt207" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-41" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-41"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-41" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 40;" id="mw8Q"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw8g">[40]</span></a></sup> and becoming the third-highest-selling album of the 1960s.<sup about="#mwt212" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-42" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-42"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-42" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 41;" id="mw8w"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw9A">[41]</span></a></sup> "I'm a Believer" was written by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neil_Diamond" title="Neil Diamond" id="mw9Q">Neil Diamond</a>. The Monkees' recording of the single hit the number-one spot on the U.S. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100" id="mw9g"><i id="mw9w">Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> chart for the week ending December 31, 1966, remaining there for seven weeks.<sup about="#mwt216" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-bb2008_43-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"bb2008"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-bb2008-43"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-bb2008-43" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 42;" id="mw-A"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw-Q">[42]</span></a></sup> "I'm a Believer" became the biggest-selling single for all of 1967.<sup about="#mwt220" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-44" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-44"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-44" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 43;" id="mw-g"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mw-w">[43]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mw_A">The Monkees' musical opportunities were open beyond their ability to capitalize. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Screen_Gems" title="Screen Gems" id="mw_Q">Screen Gems</a> held the publishing rights to a wealth of material, with the Monkees being offered the first choice of many new songs. Due to the abundance of material numerous tracks were recorded, but dozens were left unreleased until Rhino Records started releasing them through the Missing Links series of albums starting in the late 1980s. (A rumor persists that the Monkees were offered "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sugar,_Sugar" title="Sugar, Sugar" id="mw_g">Sugar, Sugar</a>" in 1967, but declined to record it. Producer and songwriter <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jeff_Barry" title="Jeff Barry" id="mw_w">Jeff Barry</a>, joint writer and composer of "Sugar, Sugar" with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Andy_Kim_(singer)" title="Andy Kim (singer)" id="mwAQA">Andy Kim</a>, has denied this, saying that the song had not even been written at the time.<sup about="#mwt225" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-45" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-45"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-45" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 44;" id="mwAQE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQI">[44]</span></a></sup>)</p>
        
        <p id="mwAQM">The Monkees' UK tour in 1967 received a chilly reception; the front pages of several UK and international music papers proclaimed that the group members did not always play their own instruments or sing the backing vocals in the studio. They were derisively dubbed the "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pre-fabrication" title="Pre-fabrication" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAQQ">Pre-Fab</a> Four" and the <i id="mwAQU"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sunday_Mirror" title="Sunday Mirror" id="mwAQY">Sunday Mirror</a></i> called them a "disgrace to the pop world."<sup about="#mwt233" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-46" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-46"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-46" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 45;" id="mwAQc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQg">[45]</span></a></sup> However, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./George_Harrison" title="George Harrison" id="mwAQk">George Harrison</a> praised the Monkees' self-produced musical attempts.<sup about="#mwt234" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-baker_32-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"baker"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-baker-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAQo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAQs">[31]</span></a></sup> Peter Tork was later one of the musicians on Harrison's album <i id="mwAQw"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wonderwall_Music" title="Wonderwall Music" id="mwAQ0">Wonderwall Music</a></i>, playing <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paul_McCartney" title="Paul McCartney" id="mwAQ4">Paul McCartney</a>'s five-string <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Banjo" title="Banjo" id="mwAQ8">banjo</a>.<sup about="#mwt238" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-47" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-47"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-47" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 46;" id="mwARA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwARE">[46]</span></a></sup> Nesmith attended the Beatles' recording session for "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./A_Day_in_the_Life" title="A Day in the Life" id="mwARI">A Day in the Life</a>" at Abbey Road Studios. At that time, he reportedly asked John Lennon, "Do you think we're a cheap imitation of the Beatles, your movies and your records?" Lennon replied, "I think you're the greatest comic talent since the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Marx_Brothers" title="Marx Brothers" id="mwARM">Marx Brothers</a>. I've never missed one of your programs".<sup about="#mwt239" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-baker_32-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"baker"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-baker-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwARQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwARU">[31]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="9" id="mwARY"><h4 id="Headquarters_and_Pisces,_Aquarius,_Capricorn_&amp;_Jones"><span id="Headquarters_and_Pisces.2C_Aquarius.2C_Capricorn_.26_Jones" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span><i id="mwARc">Headquarters</i> and <i id="mwARg">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones</i></h4>
        <p id="mwARk">With Kirshner dismissed as musical supervisor, in late February 1967 Nesmith hired former <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Turtles" title="The Turtles" id="mwARo">Turtles</a> bassist Douglas Farthing Hatlelid, who was better known by his stage name <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Chip_Douglas" title="Chip Douglas" id="mwARs">Chip Douglas</a>, to produce the next Monkees album.<sup about="#mwt241" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage80_37-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage80"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage80-37" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 36;" id="mwARw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAR0">[36]</span></a></sup> This album was to be the first Monkees album where they were the only musicians, outside of most of the bass and the horns. Douglas was responsible for both music presentation—actually leading the band and engineering recordings—and playing bass on most of <i id="mwAR4"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Headquarters_(the_Monkees_album)" title="Headquarters (the Monkees album)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAR8">Headquarters</a>.</i> This album, along with their next, <i id="mwASA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pisces,_Aquarius,_Capricorn_&amp;_Jones_Ltd." title="Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd." id="mwASE">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd.</a></i>, served as the soundtrack to the second season of the television show.</p>
        
        <p id="mwASI">In March 1967, "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Girl_I_Knew_Somewhere" title="The Girl I Knew Somewhere" id="mwASM">The Girl I Knew Somewhere</a>", composed by Nesmith and performed by Dolenz, Nesmith, Tork and bassist <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./John_London" title="John London" id="mwASQ">John London</a>, was issued as the B-side to the Monkees' third single, "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./A_Little_Bit_Me,_a_Little_Bit_You" title="A Little Bit Me, a Little Bit You" id="mwASU">A Little Bit Me, a Little Bit You</a>", and it rose to No. 39 on the charts. The A-side rose to No. 2.<sup about="#mwt243" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage96_48-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage96"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage96-48"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage96-48" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 47;" id="mwASY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwASc">[47]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwASg">Issued in May 1967, <i id="mwASk">Headquarters</i> had no songs released as singles in the United States, but it was still their third No. 1 album in a row, with many of its songs played on the second season of the television show. Having a more country-folk-rock sound than the pop outings under Kirshner, Sandoval notes in the 2007 Deluxe Edition reissue from Rhino that the album rose to No. 1 on May 24, 1967, with the Beatles' <i id="mwASo">Sgt. Pepper</i> released the following week, which moved <i id="mwASs">Headquarters</i> to the #2 spot on the charts for the next 11 weeks—the same weeks which became known by the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s" id="mwASw">counterculture</a> as the "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Summer_of_Love" title="Summer of Love" id="mwAS0">Summer of Love</a>". A selection that Dolenz wrote and composed, "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Randy_Scouse_Git" title="Randy Scouse Git" id="mwAS4">Randy Scouse Git</a>", was issued under the title "Alternate Title" (owing to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Git_(slang)" title="Git (slang)" id="mwAS8">the controversial nature of its original title</a>) as a single internationally, where it rose to No. 2 on the charts in the UK and Norway, and in the top 10 in other parts of the world.<sup about="#mwt247" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage116_49-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage116"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage116-49"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage116-49" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 48;" id="mwATA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATE">[48]</span></a></sup> Tork's "For Pete's Sake" was used as the closing theme for the television show. Nesmith continued in his country-rock leanings, adding the pedal steel guitar to three of the songs, along with contributing his self-composed countrified-rock song "Sunny Girlfriend". Tork added the banjo to the Nesmith-composed rocker "You Told Me", a song whose introduction was satirical of the Beatles' "Taxman".<sup about="#mwt248" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage97_50-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage97"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage97-50"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage97-50" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 49;" id="mwATI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATM">[49]</span></a></sup> Other notable songs are the Nesmith-composed straightforward pop-rock song "You Just May Be the One" (the only track from their peak years to feature the Monkees playing the same instruments they were shown to play on the television show), used on the television series during both seasons, along with "Shades of Gray" (with piano introduction written by Tork),<sup about="#mwt249" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage101_51-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage101"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage101-51"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage101-51" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 50;" id="mwATQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATU">[50]</span></a></sup> "Forget that Girl", and "No Time", used in the television show. The Monkees wrote five of the 12 songs on the album, plus the two tracks "Band 6" and "Zilch".</p>
        
        <p id="mwATY">The <i id="mwATc">Los Angeles Times</i>, when reviewing <i id="mwATg">Headquarters</i>, stated that "The Monkees Upgrade Album Quality" and that "The Monkees are getting better. <i id="mwATk">Headquarters</i> has more interesting songs and a better quality level [than previous albums]... None of the tracks is a throwaway... The improvement trend is laudable."<sup about="#mwt251" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage109_52-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage109"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage109-52"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage109-52" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 51;" id="mwATo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwATs">[51]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwATw">The high of <i id="mwAT0">Headquarters</i> was short-lived, however. Recording and producing as a group was Tork's major interest and he hoped that the four would continue working together as a band on future recordings, according to the liner notes of the 2007 Rhino reissue of <i id="mwAT4">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd.</i>. "Cuddly Toy" on <i id="mwAT8">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd.</i> marked the last time Dolenz, who originally played guitar before the Monkees, made a solo stand as a studio drummer.<sup about="#mwt253" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage108_53-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage108"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage108-53"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage108-53" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 52;" id="mwAUA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAUE">[52]</span></a></sup> In commentary for the DVD release of the second season of the show, Tork said that Dolenz was "incapable of repeating a triumph." Having been a drummer for one album, Dolenz lost interest in being a drummer and, indeed, he largely gave up playing instruments on Monkees recordings to session musicians like <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href='./"Fast"_Eddie_Hoh' title='"Fast" Eddie Hoh' class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUI">"Fast" Eddie Hoh</a>. (Producer Chip Douglas also had identified Dolenz's drumming as the weak point in the collective musicianship of the quartet, having to splice together multiple takes of Dolenz's "shaky" drumming for final use.)</p>
        
        <p id="mwAUM">By this point, the four did not have a common vision regarding their musical interests, with Nesmith and Jones also moving in different directions—Nesmith following his country/folk instincts and Jones reaching for Broadway-style numbers. The next three albums featured a diverse mixture of musical style influences, including country-rock, folk-rock, psychedelic rock, soul/R&amp;B, guitar rock, Broadway and English music hall sensibilities.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAUQ">At the height of their fame in 1967, they also suffered from a media backlash. Nesmith states in the 2007 Rhino reissue of <i id="mwAUU">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd.</i>, "Everybody in the press and in the hippie movement had got us into their target window as being illegitimate and not worthy of consideration as a musical force [or] certainly any kind of cultural force. We were under siege; wherever we went there was such resentment for us. We were constantly mocked and humiliated by the press. We were really gettin' beat up pretty good. We all knew what was going on inside. Kirshner had been purged. We'd gone to try to make <i id="mwAUY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Headquarters_(the_Monkees_album)" title="Headquarters (the Monkees album)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAUc">Headquarters</a></i> and found out that it was only marginally okay and that our better move was to just go back to the original songwriting and song-making strategy of the first albums except with a clear indication of how [the music] came to be... The rabid element and the hatred that was engendered is almost impossible to describe. It lingers to this day among people my own age." Tork disagreed with Nesmith's assessment of <i id="mwAUg">Headquarters</i>, stating, "I don't think the <i id="mwAUk">Pisces</i> album was as groovy to listen to as <i id="mwAUo">Headquarters</i>. Technically it was much better, but I think it suffers for that reason."<sup about="#mwt255" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage142_54-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage142"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage142-54"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage142-54" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 53;" id="mwAUs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAUw">[53]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAU0">With <i id="mwAU4"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pisces,_Aquarius,_Capricorn_&amp;_Jones_Ltd." title="Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd." id="mwAU8">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd.</a></i>, the Monkees' fourth album, they went back to making music for the television show, except that they had control over the music and which songs would be chosen. They used a mixture of themselves and session musicians on the album, including <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Wrecking_Crew_(music)" title="The Wrecking Crew (music)" id="mwAVA">the Wrecking Crew</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Louie_Shelton" title="Louie Shelton" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAVE">Louie Shelton</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Glen_Campbell" title="Glen Campbell" id="mwAVI">Glen Campbell</a>, members of <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Byrds" title="The Byrds" id="mwAVM">the Byrds</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Association" title="The Association" id="mwAVQ">the Association</a>, drummer <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href='./"Fast"_Eddie_Hoh' title='"Fast" Eddie Hoh' class="mw-redirect" id="mwAVU">"Fast" Eddie Hoh</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Lowell_George" title="Lowell George" id="mwAVY">Lowell George</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Stephen_Stills" title="Stephen Stills" id="mwAVc">Stephen Stills</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Buddy_Miles" title="Buddy Miles" id="mwAVg">Buddy Miles</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Neil_Young" title="Neil Young" id="mwAVk">Neil Young</a>—a practice that continued for all their studio albums except <i id="mwAVo"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Justus_(album)" title="Justus (album)" id="mwAVs">Justus</a></i>.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAVw">Using Chip Douglas again to produce, <i id="mwAV0">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd.</i>, released in November 1967<sup about="#mwt260" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage142_54-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage142"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage142-54" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 53;" id="mwAV4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAV8">[53]</span></a></sup> was the Monkees' fourth No. 1 album in a row, staying at No. 1 for 5 weeks,<sup about="#mwt261" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Discography_39-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Discography"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Discography-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAWA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAWE">[38]</span></a></sup> and was also their last No. 1 album. It featured the hit single "Pleasant Valley Sunday" (#3 on charts) b/w "Words" (#11 on charts), the A-side had Nesmith on electric guitar/backing vocals, Tork on piano/backing vocals, Dolenz on lead vocals and possibly guitar and Jones on backing vocals;<sup about="#mwt262" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage116_49-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage116"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage116-49" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 48;" id="mwAWI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAWM">[48]</span></a></sup> the B-side had Micky and Peter alternating lead vocals, Peter played organ, Mike played guitar, percussion, and provided backing vocals, and Davy provided percussion and backing vocals.<sup about="#mwt263" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage302_55-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage302"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage302-55"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage302-55" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 54;" id="mwAWQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAWU">[54]</span></a></sup> Other notable items about this album is that it features an early use of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Moog_synthesizer" title="Moog synthesizer" id="mwAWY">Moog synthesizer</a> on two tracks, the Nesmith-penned "Daily Nightly", along with "Star Collector". All of its songs, except for two, were featured on the Monkees' television show during the second season.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAWc">The song "What Am I Doing Hangin' 'Round?", recorded in June 1967 and featured on <i id="mwAWg">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd.</i>, is seen as a landmark in the fusion of country and rock<sup about="#mwt266" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage118_56-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage118"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage118-56"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage118-56" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 55;" id="mwAWk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAWo">[55]</span></a></sup> despite Nesmith's prior country-flavored rock songs for the Monkees. Nesmith stated, "One of the things that I really felt was honest was country-rock. I wanted to move the Monkees more into that because ... if we get closer to country music, we'll get closer to blues, and country blues, and so forth. ... It had a lot of un-country things in it: a familiar change from a I major to a VI minor—those kinds of things. So it was a little kind of a new wave country song. It didn't sound like the country songs of the time, which was Buck Owens."<sup about="#mwt267" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage118_56-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage118"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage118-56" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 55;" id="mwAWs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAWw">[55]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAW0">Their next single, "Daydream Believer" (with a piano intro written by Tork), shot to No. 1 on the charts, letting the Monkees hold the No. 1 position in the singles chart and the album chart with Pisces simultaneously.<sup about="#mwt269" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage143_57-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage143"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage143-57"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage143-57" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 56;" id="mwAW4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAW8">[56]</span></a></sup> "Daydream Believer" used the non-album track "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Goin'_Down_(The_Monkees_song)" title="Goin' Down (The Monkees song)" id="mwAXA">Goin' Down</a>" as its B-side, which featured Nesmith and Tork on guitar with Micky on lead vocals.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAXE">During their 1986 reunion, both <i id="mwAXI">Headquarters</i> and <i id="mwAXM">Pisces, Aquarius, Capricorn &amp; Jones Ltd.</i> returned to the charts for 17 weeks.<sup about="#mwt271" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Discography_39-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Discography"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Discography-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAXQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAXU">[38]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="10" id="mwAXY"><h4 id="The_Birds,_The_Bees_&amp;_The_Monkees"><span id="The_Birds.2C_The_Bees_.26_The_Monkees" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span><i id="mwAXc">The Birds, The Bees &amp; The Monkees</i></h4>
        
        <p id="mwAXg">No longer desiring to work as a group, the Monkees dropped Chip Douglas as a producer, and starting in November 1967, they largely produced their own sessions.<sup about="#mwt275" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage142_54-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage142"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage142-54" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 53;" id="mwAXk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAXo">[53]</span></a></sup> Although credited to the whole band, the songs were mostly solo efforts.<sup about="#mwt276" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage148_58-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage148"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage148-58"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage148-58" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 57;" id="mwAXs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAXw">[57]</span></a></sup> In a couple of cases, Boyce and Hart had returned from the first two albums to produce, but credit was given to the Monkees due to contractual requirements.<sup about="#mwt277" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage152_59-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage152"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage152-59"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage152-59" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 58;" id="mwAX0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAX4">[58]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAX8">Propelled by the hit singles "Daydream Believer" and "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Valleri" title="Valleri" id="mwAYA">Valleri</a>", along with Nesmith's self-penned top 40 hit "Tapioca Tundra", <i id="mwAYE"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Birds,_The_Bees_&amp;_The_Monkees" title="The Birds, The Bees &amp; The Monkees" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAYI">The Birds, The Bees &amp; The Monkees</a></i> reached No. 3 on the Billboard charts shortly after it was released in April 1968.<sup about="#mwt281" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage183_60-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage183"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage183-60"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage183-60" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 59;" id="mwAYM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYQ">[59]</span></a></sup> It was the first album released after NBC announced they were not renewing <i id="mwAYU">The Monkees</i> for a third season. The album cover—a quaint collage of items in a knickknack shelf—was chosen over the Monkees' objections. It was the last Monkees' album to be released in separate, dedicated mono and stereo mixes.<sup about="#mwt282" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage183_60-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage183"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage183-60" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 59;" id="mwAYY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYc">[59]</span></a></sup> During the 1986 reunion, it returned to the Billboard charts for 11 weeks.<sup about="#mwt283" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Discography_39-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Discography"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Discography-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAYg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAYk">[38]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="11" id="mwAYo"><h4 id="Beyond_television_and_Head">Beyond television and <i id="mwAYs">Head</i></h4>
        <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" about="#mwt284" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"More citations class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation" about="#mwt284"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div         <p id="mwAY4">As the second season of the television series was being produced, the Monkees tired of the sitcom format and refused to participate in a third season without a major overhaul. NBC was uninterested in making any changes, and so the series was cancelled in February 1968.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAY8">That same month, the Monkees began filming their feature film, <i id="mwAZA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Head_(film)" title="Head (film)" id="mwAZE">Head</a></i>. With Bob Rafelson as director and Bert Schneider as executive producer, the project was co-written and co-produced by Rafelson with a then-relatively unknown <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jack_Nicholson" title="Jack Nicholson" id="mwAZI">Jack Nicholson</a>.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAZM">The film, conceived and edited in a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Stream_of_consciousness" title="Stream of consciousness" id="mwAZQ">stream of consciousness</a> style, featured oddball <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cameo_appearance" title="Cameo appearance" id="mwAZU">cameo appearances</a> by movie stars <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Victor_Mature" title="Victor Mature" id="mwAZY">Victor Mature</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Annette_Funicello" title="Annette Funicello" id="mwAZc">Annette Funicello</a>, a young <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Teri_Garr" title="Teri Garr" id="mwAZg">Teri Garr</a> (credited as "Terry Garr"), boxer <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sonny_Liston" title="Sonny Liston" id="mwAZk">Sonny Liston</a>, famous <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Stripper" title="Stripper" id="mwAZo">stripper</a> <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Carol_Doda" title="Carol Doda" id="mwAZs">Carol Doda</a>, Green Bay Packers linebacker <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Ray_Nitschke" title="Ray Nitschke" id="mwAZw">Ray Nitschke</a>, and musician <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Frank_Zappa" title="Frank Zappa" id="mwAZ0">Frank Zappa</a>. It was filmed at <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Columbia_Pictures" title="Columbia Pictures" id="mwAZ4">Columbia Pictures</a>' <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Screen_Gems" title="Screen Gems" id="mwAZ8">Screen Gems</a> studios and on location in California, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Utah" title="Utah" id="mwAaA">Utah</a>, and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas" id="mwAaE">the Bahamas</a> between February 19 and May 17, 1968, and premiered in New York City on November 6 of that year (the film later debuted in Hollywood on November 20).</p>
        
        <p id="mwAaI">The film was the antithesis of <i id="mwAaM">The Monkees</i> television show. Rafelson and Nicholson's "Ditty Diego-War Chant" (recited at the start of the film by the group) ruthlessly parodies Boyce and Hart's "Monkees Theme". A sparse advertising campaign (with no mention of the Monkees) hurt any chances of the film doing well, and it played briefly in half-filled theaters. In the DVD commentary, Nesmith said that everyone associated with the Monkees "had gone crazy" by this time. They were each using the platform of the Monkees to push their own disparate career goals, to the detriment of the Monkees project. Nesmith added that <i id="mwAaQ">Head</i> was Rafelson and Nicholson's intentional effort to "kill" the Monkees, so that they would no longer be bothered with the matter.<sup about="#mwt288" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage219_61-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage219"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage219-61"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage219-61" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 60;" id="mwAaU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAaY">[60]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAac">A poor audience response at an August 1968 screening in Los Angeles forced the producers to edit the picture from its original 110-minute length. The 86-minute <i id="mwAag">Head</i> premiered in New York City on November 6, 1968; the film later debuted in Hollywood on November 20. It was not a commercial success. This was in part because <i id="mwAak">Head</i> comprehensively demolished the group's carefully groomed public image while the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s" id="mwAao">counterculture</a> audience they had been reaching for rejected the Monkees' efforts out of hand. Receiving mixed critical reviews and virtually non-existent box office receipts, the film succeeded in alienating the band's teenage fanbase while failing to attract a more adult audience.<sup about="#mwt294" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_62-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"LATimes"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-LATimes-62"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-LATimes-62" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 61;" id="mwAas"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAaw">[61]</span></a></sup> Rafelson and Schneider severed all ties to the band amid the bitterness that ensued over the commercial failure of <i id="mwAa0">Head</i>. At the time, Rafelson told the press, "I grooved on those four in very special ways while at the same time thinking they had absolutely no talent."<sup about="#mwt295" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage219_61-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage219"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage219-61" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 60;" id="mwAa4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAa8">[60]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAbA">Released in October 1968, the single from the album, "The Porpoise Song", is a psychedelic pop song written by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Goffin_and_King" title="Goffin and King" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAbE">Goffin and King</a>, with lead vocals from Micky Dolenz and backing vocals from Davy Jones, and it reached No. 62 on the Billboard charts<sup about="#mwt298" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage207_63-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage207"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage207-63"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage207-63" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 62;" id="mwAbI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbM">[62]</span></a></sup> and No. 26 on the Canadian <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./RPM_(magazine)" title="RPM (magazine)" id="mwAbQ">RPM charts</a>.<sup about="#mwt302" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-64" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-64"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-64" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 63;" id="mwAbU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbY">[63]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAbc">The film's soundtrack album reached No. 45 on the Billboard charts<sup about="#mwt305" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage210_65-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage210"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage210-65"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage210-65" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 64;" id="mwAbg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbk">[64]</span></a></sup> and No. 24 in Canada.<sup about="#mwt309" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-66" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-66"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-66" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 65;" id="mwAbo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAbs">[65]</span></a></sup> Nicholson assembled the album, weaving dialogue and sound effects from the film in between the songs. The six (plus "Ditty Diego") Monkees songs on the album range from psychedelic pop to straightforward rockers to Broadway rock to eastern-influenced pop to a folk-rock ballad. Although the Monkees performed "Circle Sky" live in the film, the studio version was chosen for the album. The live version was later released on various compilations, including Rhino's Missing Links series of Monkees albums. The album also includes a song from the film's composer, Ken Thorne.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAbw">The album had a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mylar" title="Mylar" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAb0">mylar</a> cover, to give it a mirror-like appearance, so that the person looking at the cover would see his own head, a play on the album title <i id="mwAb4">Head</i>.
