Peter Tork said, "That was something special... [Jack] Nicholson coordinated the record, made it up from the soundtrack. He made it different from the movie. There's a line in the movie where [Frank] Zappa says, 'That's pretty white.' Then there's another line in the movie that was not juxtaposed in the movie, but Nicholson put them together in the [soundtrack album], when Mike says, 'And the same thing goes for Christmas'... that's funny... very different from the movie... that was very important and wonderful that he assembled the record differently from the movie... It was a different artistic experience."<sup about="#mwt311" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage204_67-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage204"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage204-67"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage204-67" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 66;" id="mwAb8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAcA">[66]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAcE">Over the intervening years <i id="mwAcI">Head</i> has developed a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Cult_following" title="Cult following" id="mwAcM">cult following</a> for its innovative style and anarchic humor. Members of the Monkees, Nesmith in particular, cite the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Head_(The_Monkees_album)" title="Head (The Monkees album)" id="mwAcQ">soundtrack album</a> as one of the crowning achievements of the band.</p>
        
        </section></section><section data-mw-section-id="12" id="mwAcU"><h3 id="Later_years_and_separation">Later years and separation</h3>
        <figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAcY"><a href="./File:Monkees_Television_special_1969.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAcc"><img resource="./File:Monkees_Television_special_1969.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Monkees_Television_special_1969.jpg/220px-Monkees_Television_special_1969.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="868" data-file-height="986" data-file-type="bitmap" height="250" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Monkees_Television_special_1969.jpg/330px-Monkees_Television_special_1969.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Monkees_Television_special_1969.jpg/440px-Monkees_Television_special_1969.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element" id="mwAcg"/></a><figcaption id="mwAck">1969 television special <i id="mwAco"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./33⅓_Revolutions_per_Monkee" title="33⅓ Revolutions per Monkee" id="mwAcs">33 1/3 Revolutions Per Monkee</a></i></figcaption></figure>
        
        <section data-mw-section-id="13" id="mwAcw"><h4 id="Tork's_resignation,_Instant_Replay_and_The_Monkees_Present"><span id="Tork.27s_resignation.2C_Instant_Replay_and_The_Monkees_Present" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span>Tork's resignation, <i id="mwAc0">Instant Replay</i> and <i id="mwAc4">The Monkees Present</i></h4>
        <p id="mwAc8">Tensions within the group were increasing. Peter Tork, citing exhaustion, quit the band by buying out the last four years of his Monkees contract at $150,000 per year, equal to about $<span about="#mwt312" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"formatnum:{{Inflation|US|150000|1969|r=-4}}","function":"formatnum"},"params":{},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAdA">1,250,000</span> per year today.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_vague_or_ambiguous_time" about="#mwt314" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"when","href":"./Template:When"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"December 2023"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAdE"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Vague_or_ambiguous_time_from_December_2023" about="#mwt314"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template " style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt314" id="mwAdI"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (December 2023)">when?</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup> Tork departed shortly after the band's September-October Far East tour in December 1968 and after the band completed work on their 1969 NBC television special, <i id="mwAdM"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./33⅓_Revolutions_Per_Monkee" title="33⅓ Revolutions Per Monkee" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAdQ">33⅓ Revolutions Per Monkee</a></i> (which rehashed many of the ideas from <i id="mwAdU">Head</i>, only with the Monkees playing a strangely second-string role). In the DVD commentary for the television special, Dolenz noted that after filming was complete, Nesmith gave Tork a gold watch as a going-away present, engraved "From the guys down at work." Most of the songs from the <i id="mwAdY">33 1/3 Revolutions Per Monkee</i> TV Special were not officially released until over 40 years later, on the 2010 and 2011 Rhino Handmade Deluxe boxed sets of <i id="mwAdc">Head</i> and <i id="mwAdg">Instant Replay</i>.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAdk">Since each of the Monkees at this point were producing their own songs with very little of the other band members' involvement, they planned a future double album (eventually to be reduced to <i id="mwAdo"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Monkees_Present" title="The Monkees Present" id="mwAds">The Monkees Present</a></i>) on which each Monkee would separately produce one side of a disc.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAdw">In February 1969, the Monkees' seventh album, <i id="mwAd0"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Instant_Replay_(The_Monkees_album)" title="Instant Replay (The Monkees album)" id="mwAd4">Instant Replay</a>,</i> without Tork's involvement beyond playing guitar on "I Won't Be the Same Without Her", was released, which reached No. 32 on the charts,<sup about="#mwt319" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage226_68-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage226"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage226-68"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage226-68" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 67;" id="mwAd8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAeA">[67]</span></a></sup> and No. 45 in Canada.<sup about="#mwt323" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-69" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-69"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-69" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 68;" id="mwAeE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAeI">[68]</span></a></sup> The single from the album was "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Tear_Drop_City" title="Tear Drop City" id="mwAeM">Tear Drop City</a>", which peaked at No. 56 on the U.S. Billboard chart, No. 27 on the Canadian chart,<sup about="#mwt327" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-70" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-70"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-70" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 69;" id="mwAeQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAeU">[69]</span></a></sup> and No. 34 on the Australian chart.<sup about="#mwt328" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage227_71-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage227"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage227-71"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage227-71" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 70;" id="mwAeY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAec">[70]</span></a></sup> According to Rhino Handmade's 2011 Deluxe Edition reissue of this album, Davy Jones told <i id="mwAeg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Melody_Maker" title="Melody Maker" id="mwAek">Melody Maker</a></i>, "Half of the songs were recorded over the last three years, but there are also about six new ones." The Monkees wanted to please the original 1966 fans by offering up new recordings of some previously unreleased older styled songs, as well as gain a new audience with what they considered a more mature sound. Nesmith continued in his country-rock vein after offering straight ahead rock and experimental songs on the two prior albums.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAeo">Dolenz contributed the biggest and longest Monkees' production, "Shorty Blackwell", a song inspired by his cat of the same name.<sup about="#mwt331" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage161_72-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage161"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage161-72"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage161-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 71;" id="mwAes"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAew">[71]</span></a></sup> Dolenz called it his "feeble attempt at something to do with <i id="mwAe0">Sgt. Pepper.</i>"<sup about="#mwt332" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage161_72-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage161"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage161-72" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 71;" id="mwAe4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAe8">[71]</span></a></sup> Jones contributed an electric guitar rocker, "You and I". Both Jones and Dolenz continued their role of singing on the pop songs. Lyrically, it has a theme of being one of the Monkees' most melancholy albums.</p>
        
        <figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAfA"><a href="./File:Monkees_1969_Joey_Bishop_Show.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAfE"><img resource="./File:Monkees_1969_Joey_Bishop_Show.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Monkees_1969_Joey_Bishop_Show.jpg/220px-Monkees_1969_Joey_Bishop_Show.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1067" data-file-height="744" data-file-type="bitmap" height="153" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Monkees_1969_Joey_Bishop_Show.jpg/330px-Monkees_1969_Joey_Bishop_Show.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Monkees_1969_Joey_Bishop_Show.jpg/440px-Monkees_1969_Joey_Bishop_Show.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element" id="mwAfI"/></a><figcaption id="mwAfM">The Monkees (without Tork) performing on <i id="mwAfQ"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Joey_Bishop_Show_(talk_show)" title="The Joey Bishop Show (talk show)" id="mwAfU">The Joey Bishop Show</a></i>, backed by the Goodtimers, in 1969. The images of Jones and Dolenz were re-used for the cover art of the 1970 Monkees album <i id="mwAfY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Changes_(The_Monkees_album)" title="Changes (The Monkees album)" id="mwAfc">Changes</a></i>.</figcaption></figure><p id="mwAfg">Throughout 1969 the trio appeared as guests on television programs such as <i id="mwAfk"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Glen_Campbell_Goodtime_Hour" title="The Glen Campbell Goodtime Hour" id="mwAfo">The Glen Campbell Goodtime Hour</a></i>, <i id="mwAfs"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Johnny_Cash_Show" title="The Johnny Cash Show" id="mwAfw">The Johnny Cash Show</a></i>, <i id="mwAf0"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hollywood_Squares" title="Hollywood Squares" id="mwAf4">Hollywood Squares</a></i>, and <i id="mwAf8"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Laugh-In" title="Laugh-In" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAgA">Laugh-In</a></i> (Jones had also appeared on <i id="mwAgE">Laugh-In</i> separate from the group). The Monkees also had a contractual obligation to appear in several television commercials with <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bugs_Bunny" title="Bugs Bunny" id="mwAgI">Bugs Bunny</a> for <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Kool-Aid" title="Kool-Aid" id="mwAgM">Kool-Aid</a> drink mix as well as Post cereal box singles.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAgQ">In April 1969, the single "Someday Man" b/w "Listen to the Band" was released,<sup about="#mwt338" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage253_73-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage253"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage253-73"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage253-73" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwAgU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgY">[72]</span></a></sup> which had the unique distinction of the B-side, a Nesmith-composed country-rock song, charting higher (No. 63) than the Jones-sung A-side (No. 81).<sup about="#mwt339" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Discography_39-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Discography"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Discography-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAgc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgg">[38]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt340" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage253_73-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage253"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage253-73" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwAgk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgo">[72]</span></a></sup> In Canada, "Someday Man" was No. 74<sup about="#mwt344" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-74" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-74"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-74" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 73;" id="mwAgs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAgw">[73]</span></a></sup> and "Listen to the Band" reached No. 53.<sup about="#mwt348" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-75" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-75"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-75" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 74;" id="mwAg0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAg4">[74]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAg8">The final album with Michael Nesmith from the Monkees' original incarnation was their eighth album, <i id="mwAhA"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Monkees_Present" title="The Monkees Present" id="mwAhE">The Monkees Present</a></i>, released in October 1969, which peaked at No. 100 on the Billboard charts.<sup about="#mwt353" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage253_73-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage253"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage253-73" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 72;" id="mwAhI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAhM">[72]</span></a></sup> It included the Nesmith composed country-rock singles "Listen to the Band" and "Good Clean Fun" (released in September 1969)<sup about="#mwt354" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage251_76-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage251"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage251-76"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage251-76" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 75;" id="mwAhQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAhU">[75]</span></a></sup>(No. 80 Canada<sup about="#mwt358" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-77" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-77"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-77" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 76;" id="mwAhY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAhc">[76]</span></a></sup>) Other notable songs include the Dolenz composition "Little Girl", which featured Louie Shelton on electric guitar, joining Micky on acoustic guitar,<sup about="#mwt359" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage249_78-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage249"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage249-78"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage249-78" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 77;" id="mwAhg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAhk">[77]</span></a></sup> along with "Mommy and Daddy" (B-side to the "Good Clean Fun" single) in which he sang about America's treatment of the Native Americans and drug abuse, and in an earlier take, released on Rhino Handmade's 2011 Deluxe Edition of <i id="mwAho">Instant Replay</i>, sang about JFK's assassination and the Vietnam war. Jones collaborated with Bill Chadwick on some slower ballads, along with releasing a couple of older upbeat songs from 1966.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAhs">In the summer of 1969, the three remaining Monkees embarked on a tour with the backing of the soul band Sam and the Goodtimers. Concerts for this tour were longer sets than their earlier performances tours, with many shows running over two hours. Although the tour was met with some positive critical reception (<i id="mwAhw">Billboard</i> in particular praised it), other critics were not favorable of the mixing of the Monkees' pop music with the Goodtimers' R&amp;B approach. Toward the end of the tour, some dates were canceled due to poor ticket sales. The tour failed to re-establish the band commercially, with no single entering the Top 40 in 1969. Dolenz remarked that the tour "was like kicking a dead horse. The phenomenon had peaked".<sup about="#mwt364" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-79" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-79"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-79" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 78;" id="mwAh0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAh4">[78]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="14" id="mwAh8"><h4 id="Nesmith's_resignation,_Changes_and_disbandment"><span id="Nesmith.27s_resignation.2C_Changes_and_disbandment" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span>Nesmith's resignation, <i id="mwAiA">Changes</i> and disbandment</h4>
        <p id="mwAiE">On April 14, 1970, Nesmith joined Dolenz and Jones for the last time as part of the original incarnation of the Monkees to film a <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Kool-Aid" title="Kool-Aid" id="mwAiI">Kool-Aid</a> commercial (with the then-newly introduced <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nerf" title="Nerf" id="mwAiM">Nerf</a> balls, thrown around a mock living room by the trio, available as a premium for Kool-Aid labels),<sup about="#mwt367" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage267_80-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage267"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage267-80"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage267-80" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 79;" id="mwAiQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAiU">[79]</span></a></sup> with Nesmith leaving the group to continue recording songs with his own country-rock group called Michael Nesmith &amp; <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_First_National_Band" title="The First National Band" id="mwAiY">the First National Band</a>, which he had started recording with on February 10, 1970.<sup about="#mwt368" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage263_81-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage263"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage263-81"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage263-81" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 80;" id="mwAic"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAig">[80]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAik">Nesmith's departure left Dolenz and Jones to record the bubblegum pop album <i id="mwAio"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Changes_(The_Monkees_album)" title="Changes (The Monkees album)" id="mwAis">Changes</a></i> as the ninth and final album by the Monkees released during its original incarnation. By this time, Colgems was hardly putting any effort into the project, and they sent Dolenz and Jones to New York for the <i id="mwAiw">Changes</i> sessions, to be produced by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jeff_Barry" title="Jeff Barry" id="mwAi0">Jeff Barry</a>. In comments for the liner notes of the 1994 re-release of <i id="mwAi4">Changes</i>, Jones said that he felt they had been tricked into recording an "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Andy_Kim_(singer)" title="Andy Kim (singer)" id="mwAi8">Andy Kim</a> album" under the Monkees name. Except for the two singers' vocal performances, <i id="mwAjA">Changes</i> is the only album that fails to win any significant praise from critics looking back 40 years to the Monkees' recording output.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_unsourced_statements" about="#mwt372" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"citation needed","href":"./Template:Citation_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"November 2022"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAjE"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_unsourced_statements_from_November_2022" about="#mwt372"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt372" id="mwAjI"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup> The album spawned the single "Oh My My", which was accompanied by a music film promo (produced/directed by Dolenz). Dolenz contributed one of his own compositions, "Midnight Train", which was used in the re-runs of the Monkees TV series. The "Oh My My" b/w "I Love You Better" single from the <i id="mwAjM">Changes</i> album was the last single issued under the Monkees name in the United States until 1986.<sup about="#mwt373" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage266_82-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage266"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage266-82"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage266-82" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 81;" id="mwAjQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAjU">[81]</span></a></sup> Originally released in June 1970,<sup about="#mwt374" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage270_83-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage270"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage270-83"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage270-83" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 82;" id="mwAjY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAjc">[82]</span></a></sup> <i id="mwAjg">Changes</i> failed to chart in Billboard's Top 200 until the Monkees' 1986 reunion, when it stayed on the charts for 4 weeks.<sup about="#mwt375" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Discography_39-5" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Discography"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Discography-39" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 38;" id="mwAjk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAjo">[38]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAjs">September 22, 1970 marked the final recording session by the Monkees before the band broke up. On that date, Jones and Dolenz recorded "Do It in the Name of Love" and "Lady Jane".<sup about="#mwt379" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage276_84-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage276"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-SandovalPage276-84"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage276-84" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 83;" id="mwAjw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAj0">[83]</span></a></sup> The single was not mixed until February 19, 1971, and was released later that year as a single.<sup about="#mwt380" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage84_20-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage84"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage84-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mwAj4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAj8">[19]</span></a></sup> The two remaining Monkees then lost the rights to use the name in several countries, the U.S. included. The single was not credited to the Monkees in the U.S., but to a misspelled "Mickey Dolenz and Davy Jones",<sup about="#mwt381" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-SandovalPage84_20-4" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"SandovalPage84"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-SandovalPage84-20" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 19;" id="mwAkA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAkE">[19]</span></a></sup> although in Japan it was issued under the Monkees' name.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAkI">Both Jones and Dolenz released multiple singles as solo artists in the years following the original breakup of the Monkees. The duo continued to tour throughout most of the 1970s.</p>
        
        </section></section><section data-mw-section-id="15" id="mwAkM"><h3 id="Reunions_and_revivals">Reunions and revivals</h3>
        
        <section data-mw-section-id="16" id="mwAkQ"><h4 id="Dolenz,_Jones,_Boyce_&amp;_Hart"><span id="Dolenz.2C_Jones.2C_Boyce_.26_Hart" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span>Dolenz, Jones, Boyce &amp; Hart</h4>
        <p id="mwAkU">Partly because of repeats of the television series <i id="mwAkY">The Monkees</i> on Saturday mornings and in syndication, <i id="mwAkc"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Monkees_Greatest_Hits" title="The Monkees Greatest Hits" id="mwAkg">The Monkees Greatest Hits</a></i> charted in 1976. The LP, issued by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Arista_Records" title="Arista Records" id="mwAkk">Arista Records</a> (who by this time had possession of the Monkees' master tapes courtesy of their corporate owner, Screen Gems), was actually a re-packaging of an earlier (1972) compilation LP called <i id="mwAko">Refocus</i> that had been issued by Arista's previous label imprint, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Bell_Records" title="Bell Records" id="mwAks">Bell Records</a>. Dolenz and Jones took advantage of this, joining ex-Monkees songwriters <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Boyce_and_Hart" title="Boyce and Hart" id="mwAkw">Tommy Boyce and Bobby Hart</a> to tour the United States. From 1975 to 1977, as the "Golden Hits of the Monkees" show ("The Guys who Wrote 'Em and the Guys who Sang 'Em!"), they successfully performed in smaller venues such as state fairs and amusement parks. The tour also made stops in Japan, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Thailand" title="Thailand" id="mwAk0">Thailand</a>, Hong Kong and Singapore. An album of new material was released under the name <i id="mwAk4"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Dolenz,_Jones,_Boyce_&amp;_Hart" title="Dolenz, Jones, Boyce &amp; Hart" id="mwAk8">Dolenz, Jones, Boyce &amp; Hart</a></i>. Nesmith had not been interested in a reunion, and Tork could not be located in time to be invited.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_unsourced_statements" about="#mwt382" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"citation needed","href":"./Template:Citation_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"January 2024"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAlA"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_unsourced_statements_from_January_2024" about="#mwt382"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt382" id="mwAlE"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAlI">A Christmas single (credited to Micky Dolenz, Davy Jones and Peter Tork due to legal reasons) was produced by Chip Douglas and released on his own label in 1976. The single featured Douglas' and Howard Kaylan's "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Christmas_Is_My_Time_of_Year" title="Christmas Is My Time of Year" id="mwAlM">Christmas Is My Time of Year</a>" (originally recorded by a 1960s group Christmas Spirit), with a B-side of Irving Berlin's "<a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./White_Christmas_(song)" title="White Christmas (song)" id="mwAlQ">White Christmas</a>" (Douglas released a remixed version of the single, with additional overdubbed instruments, in 1986). This was the first (albeit unofficial) Monkees single since 1971. Tork also joined Dolenz, Jones, Boyce &amp; Hart on stage at <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Disneyland" title="Disneyland" id="mwAlU">Disneyland</a> in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Anaheim,_California" title="Anaheim, California" id="mwAlY">Anaheim, California</a> on July 4, 1976, and also joined Dolenz and Jones on stage at the Starwood in Hollywood in 1977.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAlc">In a 1977 interview, Nesmith falsely claimed that the Monkees outsold the Beatles and the Rolling Stones combined in 1967.<sup about="#mwt389" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-85" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-85"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-85" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 84;" id="mwAlg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAlk">[84]</span></a></sup> This inaccurate information was later repeated in newspapers and magazines.<sup about="#mwt393" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-86" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-86"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-86" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 85;" id="mwAlo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAls">[85]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt397" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-87" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-87"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-87" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 86;" id="mwAlw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAl0">[86]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="17" id="mwAl4"><h4 id="MTV_and_Nickelodeon_reignite_Monkeemania">MTV and Nickelodeon reignite Monkeemania</h4>
        <p id="mwAl8">Brushed off by critics during their heyday in the late 1960s as manufactured and lacking talent, the Monkees experienced a critical and commercial renaissance two decades later. A Monkees TV show marathon ("Pleasant Valley Sunday") was broadcast on February 23, 1986, on MTV. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Nickelodeon" title="Nickelodeon" id="mwAmA">Nickelodeon</a> began to run the Monkees' old television series daily. Such promotion helped to resurrect a smaller version of Monkeemania, leading to tour dates that grew from smaller to larger venues and became one of the biggest live acts of 1986 and 1987.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAmE">In February and March 1986, Tork and Jones played together in Australia. Then in May, Dolenz, Jones, and Tork announced a "20th Anniversary Tour" and began playing North America in June. In September 1988, the three rejoined to play in Australia, Europe, and North America, with that string of tours ending in September 1989.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAmI">The Monkees' original albums also began selling again, and a new greatest hits collection was issued, reaching platinum status.<sup about="#mwt402" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-88" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-88"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-88" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 87;" id="mwAmM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAmQ">[87]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAmU">By this point, Nesmith was more amenable to a reunion, but was forced to sit out most projects because of prior commitments to his Pacific Arts video production company. However, he did appear with the band in a 1986 Christmas medley music video for MTV, and appeared on stage with Dolenz, Jones, and Tork at the Greek Theatre, in Los Angeles, on September 7, 1986. Nesmith again returned at the Universal Amphitheatre (Los Angeles) show on July 10, 1989, and took part in a 1989 dedication ceremony at the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hollywood_Walk_of_Fame" title="Hollywood Walk of Fame" id="mwAmY">Hollywood Walk of Fame</a>.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAmc">The sudden revival of the Monkees in 1986 helped move the first official Monkees single since 1971, "That Was Then, This Is Now", to the No. 20 position in <i id="mwAmg">Billboard</i> Magazine. The success, however, was not without controversy. Jones had declined to sing on the track, recorded along with two other new songs included in a compilation album, <i id="mwAmk"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Then_&amp;_Now..._The_Best_of_The_Monkees" title="Then &amp; Now... The Best of The Monkees" id="mwAmo">Then &amp; Now... The Best of The Monkees</a></i>. Some copies of the single and album credit the new songs to "the Monkees", while others credit it to "Micky Dolenz and Peter Tork (of the Monkees)". These recordings were the source of some personal friction between Jones and the others during the 1986 tour; Jones typically left the stage when the new songs were performed. However, Jones did participate in the follow-up album, 1987's <i id="mwAms"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pool_It!" title="Pool It!" id="mwAmw">Pool It!</a></i>.</p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="18" id="mwAm0"><h4 id="New_Monkees">New Monkees</h4>
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" about="#mwt403" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"main","href":"./Template:Main"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"New Monkees"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAm4"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" about="#mwt403" id="mwAm8">Main article: <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./New_Monkees" title="New Monkees">New Monkees</a></div>
        <p id="mwAnA">In 1987, a new television series called <i id="mwAnE">New Monkees</i> appeared. Other than centering on a boy band quartet, it bore no resemblance to the earlier series or group. The <i id="mwAnI">New Monkees</i> left the air after 13 episodes.</p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="19" id="mwAnM"><h4 id="1990s_reunions">1990s reunions</h4>
        <p id="mwAnQ">In the 1990s, the Monkees continued to record new material. The band also re-issued all its original LPs on CD. Most of them included between three and six bonus tracks of previously unreleased songs or alternate takes; the first editions came with collectable trading cards.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAnU">The Monkees' eleventh album    <i id="mwAnY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Justus_(album)" title="Justus (album)" id="mwAnc">Justus</a></i> was released in 1996. It was the first album since 1968 on which all four original members performed and produced, and it would be the last studio album in which all four Monkees directly participated (Jones's death would necessitate the use of archival recordings on later albums). <i id="mwAng">Justus</i> was produced by the Monkees, and all of its songs were written by one or two of the four Monkees. The album was recorded using only the four Monkees for all instruments and vocals, which was the inspiration for the album title and spelling (<i id="mwAnk">Justus</i> = Just Us).</p>
        
        <p id="mwAno">The trio of Dolenz, Jones, and Tork reunited again for a successful 30th anniversary tour of American amphitheaters in 1996. Nesmith joined them onstage in Los Angeles to promote the new songs from <i id="mwAns">Justus</i>. For the first time since the brief 1986 reunion, Nesmith returned to the concert stage for a tour of the United Kingdom in 1997, highlighted by two sold-out concerts at <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wembley_Arena" title="Wembley Arena" id="mwAnw">Wembley Arena</a> in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wembley_Park" title="Wembley Park" id="mwAn0">Wembley Park</a>, London. In 1967, the Monkees had been the first group to headline on their own at the Empire Pool, as the Arena was then called.<sup about="#mwt410" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-89" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-89"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-89" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 88;" id="mwAn4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAn8">[88]</span></a></sup> This was followed by a 1997 US tour featuring Tork, Jones, and Dolenz.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAoA">The full quartet also appeared in an ABC television special entitled <i id="mwAoE"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Hey,_Hey,_It's_the_Monkees" title="Hey, Hey, It's the Monkees" id="mwAoI">Hey, Hey, It's the Monkees</a></i>, which was written and directed by Nesmith and spoofed the original series that had made them famous. Following the UK tour, Nesmith declined to continue future performances with the Monkees, having faced harsh criticism from the British music press for his deteriorating musicianship.<link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:All_articles_with_unsourced_statements" about="#mwt411" typeof="mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"citation needed","href":"./Template:Citation_needed"},"params":{"date":{"wt":"November 2022"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAoM"/><link rel="mw:PageProp/Category" href="./Category:Articles_with_unsourced_statements_from_November_2022" about="#mwt411"/><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;" about="#mwt411" id="mwAoQ"><span typeof="mw:Entity">[</span><i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i><span typeof="mw:Entity">]</span></sup> Tork noted in a DVD commentary that in 1966, Nesmith had learned "a reasonably good version of the famous 'Last Train to Clarksville' guitar lick". However, Tork indicated that Nesmith was no longer able to play that guitar lick, so Tork took over the lead guitar parts.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAoU">Nesmith's departure from the tour was acrimonious. Jones was quoted by the <i id="mwAoY">Los Angeles Times</i> as complaining that Nesmith "made a new album with us. He toured Great Britain with us. Then all of a sudden, he's not here. Later, I hear rumors he's writing a script for our next movie. Oh, really? That's bloody news to me. He's always been this aloof, inaccessible person... the fourth part of the jigsaw puzzle that never quite fit in."<sup about="#mwt416" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-90" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-90"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-90" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 89;" id="mwAoc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAog">[89]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="20" id="mwAok"><h4 id="2000s_reunions">2000s reunions</h4>
        <p id="mwAoo">After the 1997 tours, the group took another hiatus until they once again reunited in 2001 to tour the United States. However, this tour was also accompanied by public sniping. Dolenz and Jones had announced that they had "fired" Tork for his constant complaining and threatening to quit. Tork was quoted as confirming this, as well as stating that he wanted to tour with his own band, Shoe Suede Blues. Tork told WENN News that he "couldn't handle the backstage problems"; he added that because he was a recovering alcoholic, he was troubled by the overindulgence in alcohol by other members of the tour crew.<sup about="#mwt422" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-91" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-91"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-91" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 90;" id="mwAos"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAow">[90]</span></a></sup> Tork later stated in 2011 that alcohol played only a small role in his 2001 departure. He added, "I take full responsibility for the backstage problems on the 2001 tour. We were getting along pretty well until I had a meltdown. I ticked the other guys off good and proper... I really just behaved inappropriately, honestly. I apologized to them."<sup about="#mwt426" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-92" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-92"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-92" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 91;" id="mwAo0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAo4">[91]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAo8">Jones and Dolenz went on to tour the United Kingdom in 2002, but Tork declined to participate. Jones and Dolenz toured the United States one more time as a duo in 2002, and then split to concentrate on their own individual projects. With different Monkees citing different reasons, the group chose not to mark their 40th anniversary in 2006.</p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="21" id="mwApA"><h4 id="45th_anniversary_tour_and_Jones's_death"><span id="45th_anniversary_tour_and_Jones.27s_death" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span>45th anniversary tour and Jones's death</h4>
        <p id="mwApE"><i id="mwApI"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./An_Evening_with_The_Monkees:_The_45th_Anniversary_Tour" title="An Evening with The Monkees: The 45th Anniversary Tour" id="mwApM">An Evening with The Monkees: The 45th Anniversary Tour</a></i> (without Nesmith) commenced on May 12, 2011, in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Liverpool" title="Liverpool" id="mwApQ">Liverpool</a>, England,<sup about="#mwt434" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-93" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-93"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-93" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 92;" id="mwApU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwApY">[92]</span></a></sup> before moving to North America in June and July for a total of 43 performances.<sup about="#mwt438" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-94" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-94"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-94" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 93;" id="mwApc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwApg">[93]</span></a></sup> Monkees biographer <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Andrew_Sandoval" title="Andrew Sandoval" id="mwApk">Andrew Sandoval</a> noted, "Once they hit the stage, the old magic was apparent. For the next three months...[they brought] the music and memories to fans in the band's grandest stage show in decades".<sup about="#mwt439" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Sandoval_15-1" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Sandoval"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Sandoval-15" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 14;" id="mwApo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAps">[14]</span></a></sup> The tour grossed approximately $4 million.<sup about="#mwt443" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-95" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-95"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-95" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 94;" id="mwApw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAp0">[94]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAp4">On August 8, 2011, the band canceled ten last-minute shows due to what was initially reported as "internal group issues and conflicts",<sup about="#mwt449" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-96" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-96"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-96" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 95;" id="mwAp8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqA">[95]</span></a></sup> though Tork later confirmed "there were some business affairs that couldn't be coordinated correctly. We hit a glitch and there was just this weird dislocation at one point".<sup about="#mwt453" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-97" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-97"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-97" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 96;" id="mwAqE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqI">[96]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAqM">Jones clarified that "the (45th Anniversary) tour was only supposed to go until July. And it was great, the best time we've had because we're all on the same page now. We jelled onstage and off. But then more dates were being added. And more... Some of these shows were 2<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" about="#mwt455" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"fraction","href":"./Template:Fraction"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"1"},"2":{"wt":"2"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAqQ">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" about="#mwt455" id="mwAqU"><span class="num">1</span><span typeof="mw:Entity">⁄</span><span class="den">2</span></span> hours long... The audiences were great. But, let's face it, we're not kids."<sup about="#mwt461" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-98" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-98"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-98" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 97;" id="mwAqY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqc">[97]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAqg">The 45th anniversary tour was the last Monkees tour with Jones, who died of a heart attack at age 66 on February 29, 2012.<sup about="#mwt467" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-99" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-99"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-99" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 98;" id="mwAqk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqo">[98]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt471" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-WPTV_100-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"WPTV"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-WPTV-100"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-WPTV-100" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 99;" id="mwAqs"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAqw">[99]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="22" id="mwAq0"><h4 id="Reunion_with_Nesmith">Reunion with Nesmith</h4>
        <p id="mwAq4">On August 8, 2012, the surviving trio announced a series of U.S. shows for November and December, commencing in <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Escondido,_California" title="Escondido, California" id="mwAq8">Escondido, California</a> and concluding in New York City. The brief tour marked the first time Nesmith performed with the Monkees since 1997.<sup about="#mwt474" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-rhino2012tour_101-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"rhino2012tour"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-rhino2012tour-101" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 100;" id="mwArA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwArE">[100]</span></a></sup> Jones's memory was honored throughout the shows via recordings and video. During one point, the band went quiet and a recording of Jones singing "I Wanna Be Free" played while footage of him was screening behind the band. For Jones's signature song, "Daydream Believer", Dolenz said that the band had discussed who should sing the song and had concluded that it should be the fans, saying "It doesn't belong to us anymore. It belongs to you."<sup about="#mwt478" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-RSkickoff_102-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"RSkickoff"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-RSkickoff-102"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-RSkickoff-102" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 101;" id="mwArI"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwArM">[101]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwArQ">The fall 2012 tour was very well received by both fans and critics, resulting in the band's scheduling a 24-date summer tour for 2013. Dubbed "A Midsummer's Night with the Monkees", the concerts also featured Nesmith, Dolenz, and Tork. "The reaction to the last tour was euphoric", Dolenz told <i id="mwArU">Rolling Stone</i> magazine. "It was pretty apparent there was a demand for another one."<sup about="#mwt483" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-RS2013tour_103-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"RS2013tour"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-RS2013tour-103"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-RS2013tour-103" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 102;" id="mwArY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwArc">[102]</span></a></sup> A third tour with Nesmith followed in 2014.</p>
        
        <p id="mwArg">In 2014, the Monkees were inducted into the Pop Music Hall of Fame at the 2014 Monkees Convention.<sup about="#mwt488" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-104" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-104"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-104" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 103;" id="mwArk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAro">[103]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="23" id="mwArs"><h4 id="Good_Times!_and_50th_anniversary"><span id="Good_Times.21_and_50th_anniversary" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span><i id="mwArw">Good Times!</i> and 50th anniversary</h4>
        <p id="mwAr0">Dolenz and Tork toured as the Monkees in 2015 without Nesmith's participation. Nesmith stated that he was busy with other ventures, although Dolenz said that he was welcome to join them.<sup about="#mwt493" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-105" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-105"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-105" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 104;" id="mwAr4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAr8">[104]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAsA">In February 2016, Dolenz announced that the Monkees would be releasing a new album, titled <i id="mwAsE"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Good_Times!" title="Good Times!" id="mwAsI">Good Times!</a></i>, as a celebration of their 50th anniversary. <i id="mwAsM">Good Times!</i> featured contributions by all three surviving members, as well as a posthumous contribution from Jones through vocals he had recorded in the 1960s.<sup about="#mwt499" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-106" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-106"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-106" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 105;" id="mwAsQ"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAsU">[105]</span></a></sup> The album was released in May 2016 to considerable success, reaching No. 14 on the Billboard 200<sup about="#mwt503" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-107" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-107"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-107" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 106;" id="mwAsY"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAsc">[106]</span></a></sup> and receiving generally favorable reviews.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAsg">With the release of the album, the band, featuring Dolenz and Tork, commenced their 50th anniversary tour. Nesmith did not participate in most of the tour, again citing other commitments. He did, however, make a few appearances throughout the summer of 2016, appearing virtually via <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Skype" title="Skype" id="mwAsk">Skype</a> to perform "Papa Gene's Blues" at one concert and in person for a four-song encore at another. In September, he replaced Tork on the tour for two dates while Tork attended to a family emergency. After Tork returned to the tour, Nesmith performed with the band for a concert at the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Pantages_Theatre_(Hollywood)" title="Pantages Theatre (Hollywood)" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAso">Pantages Theatre</a> in Hollywood on September 16, which he stated would likely be his final concert appearance with the Monkees.<sup about="#mwt508" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-108" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-108"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-108" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 107;" id="mwAss"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAsw">[107]</span></a></sup> Dolenz and Tork's tour announced dates to the end of the year, including concerts in Australia and New Zealand.</p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="24" id="mwAs0"><h4 id="The_Mike_and_Micky_Show,_Christmas_Party,_and_Tork's_death"><span id="The_Mike_and_Micky_Show.2C_Christmas_Party.2C_and_Tork.27s_death" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span>The Mike and Micky Show, <i id="mwAs4">Christmas Party</i>, and Tork's death</h4>
        <p id="mwAs8">On February 20, 2018, a new tour was announced as "The Monkees Present: The Mike and Micky Show", their first tour as a duo; Tork was unable to participate due to health problems (a fact that was not revealed until after his death). Though the pair played Monkees music and promoted the tour under the Monkees banner, Dolenz and Nesmith respected Tork's absence by insisting that the shows be billed as a separate duo rather than being billed as official Monkees shows. Nesmith stated, "There's no pretense there about Micky and I being the Monkees. We're not."<sup about="#mwt513" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-109" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-109"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-109" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 108;" id="mwAtA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtE">[108]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAtI">The tour was cut short in June 2018, with four shows left unplayed, due to Nesmith undergoing quadruple bypass heart surgery, following a health issue that had persisted since early in the tour. After a month-long stay in the hospital, he and Dolenz announced March 2019 as make-up dates for the missed shows,<sup about="#mwt518" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-110" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-110"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-110" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 109;" id="mwAtM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtQ">[109]</span></a></sup> and the tour was later extended to include Australia and New Zealand.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAtU">The Monkees released a Christmas album, <i id="mwAtY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Christmas_Party_(The_Monkees_album)" title="Christmas Party (The Monkees album)" id="mwAtc">Christmas Party</a></i>,<sup about="#mwt524" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-111" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-111"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-111" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 110;" id="mwAtg"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAtk">[110]</span></a></sup> on October 12, 2018. The album features a mix of holiday standards and original songs written by contemporary artists. In addition to newly recorded material from the three surviving Monkees, two previously recorded songs featuring vocals from Jones were also included.<sup about="#mwt528" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-112" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-112"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-112" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 111;" id="mwAto"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAts">[111]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAtw">Tork died of cancer on February 21, 2019.<sup about="#mwt533" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-113" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-113"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-113" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 112;" id="mwAt0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAt4">[112]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section><section data-mw-section-id="25" id="mwAt8"><h4 id="Farewell_tour_and_Nesmith's_death"><span id="Farewell_tour_and_Nesmith.27s_death" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span>Farewell tour and Nesmith's death</h4>
        <p id="mwAuA">Following the success of the Mike and Micky Show, Dolenz and Nesmith announced a follow-up tour, An Evening with the Monkees, to begin in early 2020.<sup about="#mwt538" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-114" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-114"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-114" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 113;" id="mwAuE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAuI">[113]</span></a></sup> The tour was delayed, however, due the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./COVID-19_pandemic_in_the_United_States" title="COVID-19 pandemic in the United States" id="mwAuM">COVID-19 pandemic</a>. It was announced on May 4, 2021, that the rescheduled dates would be billed as a farewell tour. "The Monkees Farewell Tour" consisted of over 40 dates in the US from September to November; because of restrictions due to the ongoing COVID-19 pandemic, they were unable to play shows in Canada, the UK or Australia. The final date and final show for the Monkees Farewell Tour was held on November 14, 2021, at the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Greek_Theatre_(Los_Angeles)" title="Greek Theatre (Los Angeles)" id="mwAuQ">Greek Theatre</a> in Los Angeles.<sup about="#mwt542" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-115" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-115"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-115" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 114;" id="mwAuU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAuY">[114]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt546" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-116" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-116"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-116" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 115;" id="mwAuc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAug">[115]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAuk">In May 2021, Dolenz released a solo album, <i id="mwAuo">Dolenz Sings Nesmith</i>, featuring songs written by Nesmith and produced by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Christian_Nesmith" title="Christian Nesmith" id="mwAus">Christian Nesmith</a>.<sup about="#mwt551" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-variety.com_117-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"variety.com"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-variety.com-117"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-variety.com-117" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 116;" id="mwAuw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAu0">[116]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAu4">Nesmith died of heart failure on December 10, 2021, less than a month after the final date of the 2021 tour.<sup about="#mwt557" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-rsnesmithdeath_118-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"rsnesmithdeath"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-rsnesmithdeath-118"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-rsnesmithdeath-118" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 117;" id="mwAu8"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAvA">[117]</span></a></sup> Nesmith's death leaves Dolenz as the only surviving member of the Monkees. Tributes to Nesmith from other musicians, fans, and Dolenz were posted on social media.<sup about="#mwt561" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-119" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-119"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-119" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 118;" id="mwAvE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAvI">[118]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section></section><section data-mw-section-id="26" id="mwAvM"><h3 id="Micky_Dolenz">Micky Dolenz</h3>
        
        <section data-mw-section-id="27" id="mwAvQ"><h4 id='"The_Monkees_Celebrated_by_Micky_Dolenz"_tour'><span id=".22The_Monkees_Celebrated_by_Micky_Dolenz.22_tour" typeof="mw:FallbackId"></span>"The Monkees Celebrated by Micky Dolenz" tour</h4>
        <figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb" id="mwAvU"><a href="./File:Dolenz_&amp;_Band_2022.jpg" class="mw-file-description" id="mwAvY"><img resource="./File:Dolenz_&amp;_Band_2022.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Dolenz_%26_Band_2022.jpg/220px-Dolenz_%26_Band_2022.jpg" decoding="async" data-file-width="1099" data-file-height="824" data-file-type="bitmap" height="165" width="220" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Dolenz_%26_Band_2022.jpg/330px-Dolenz_%26_Band_2022.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Dolenz_%26_Band_2022.jpg/440px-Dolenz_%26_Band_2022.jpg 2x" class="mw-file-element" id="mwAvc"/></a><figcaption id="mwAvg">Dolenz performing on "The Monkees Celebrated by Micky Dolenz" tour, 2022</figcaption></figure>
        
        <p id="mwAvk">In early 2022, Dolenz announced that he would embark on a "special series of concert dates in April 2022. Honoring the contributions of his bandmates – the late Davy Jones, Mike Nesmith &amp; Peter Tork – in song and with personal multimedia footage of the legendary performers". Monkees manager and tour manager Andrew Sandoval stated that "We will be fully delving into The Monkees' songbook, as well as Micky's personal archive of films and photos to create a unique evening of memories..."<sup about="#mwt567" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-120" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-120"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-120" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 119;" id="mwAvo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAvs">[119]</span></a></sup> The official tour was set to start on April 5, 2022, with a pre-tour performance on "The Beach Boys Good Vibrations Cruise".<sup about="#mwt571" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-121" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-121"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-121" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 120;" id="mwAvw"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAv0">[120]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        </section></section></section><section data-mw-section-id="28" id="mwAv4"><h2 id="Impact_and_legacy">Impact and legacy</h2>
        <p id="mwAv8">The Monkees, selected specifically to appeal to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Youth_marketing" title="Youth marketing" id="mwAwA">youth market</a> as American television's response to the Beatles<sup about="#mwt576" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-122" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-122"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-122" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 121;" id="mwAwE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAwI">[121]</span></a></sup> with their manufactured <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Persona" title="Persona" id="mwAwM">personae</a> and carefully produced singles, are seen as an original precursor to the modern proliferation of studio and corporation-created bands. But this critical reputation has softened somewhat, with the recognition that the Monkees were neither the first manufactured group nor unusual in this respect. The Monkees also frequently contributed their own songwriting efforts on their albums and saw their musical skills improve. They ultimately became a self-directed group, playing their own instruments and writing many of their own songs.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAwQ"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Andrew_Sandoval" title="Andrew Sandoval" id="mwAwU">Andrew Sandoval</a> wrote the following in <i id="mwAwY">The Hollywood Reporter</i>:</p>
        
        <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1211633275" about="#mwt578" typeof="mw:Extension/templatestyles mw:Transclusion" data-mw='{"parts":[{"template":{"target":{"wt":"blockquote","href":"./Template:Blockquote"},"params":{"1":{"wt":"[The Monkees] pioneered the music video format and paved the way for every boy band that followed in their wake, from [[New Kids on the Block]] to [[&apos;N Sync]] to the [[Jonas Brothers]], while Davy set the stage for future teen idols [[David Cassidy]] and [[Justin Bieber]]. As pop stars go, you would be hard pressed to find a successful artist who didn&apos;t take a page from the Monkees&apos; playbook, even generations later. Monkee money also enabled Rafelson and Schneider to finance &apos;&apos;Easy Rider&apos;&apos; and &apos;&apos;[[Five Easy Pieces]]&apos;&apos;, which made Jack Nicholson a star. In fact, the Monkees series was the opening salvo in a revolution that brought on the New Hollywood cinema, an influence rarely acknowledged but no less impactful.&lt;ref name=\"Sandoval\" />"}},"i":0}}]}' id="mwAwc"/><blockquote class="templatequote " about="#mwt578" id="mwAwg"><p>[The Monkees] pioneered the music video format and paved the way for every boy band that followed in their wake, from <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./New_Kids_on_the_Block" title="New Kids on the Block">New Kids on the Block</a> to <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./'N_Sync" title="'N Sync" class="mw-redirect">'N Sync</a> to the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Jonas_Brothers" title="Jonas Brothers">Jonas Brothers</a>, while Davy set the stage for future teen idols <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./David_Cassidy" title="David Cassidy">David Cassidy</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Justin_Bieber" title="Justin Bieber">Justin Bieber</a>. As pop stars go, you would be hard pressed to find a successful artist who didn't take a page from the Monkees' playbook, even generations later. Monkee money also enabled Rafelson and Schneider to finance <i>Easy Rider</i> and <i><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Five_Easy_Pieces" title="Five Easy Pieces">Five Easy Pieces</a></i>, which made Jack Nicholson a star. In fact, the Monkees series was the opening salvo in a revolution that brought on the New Hollywood cinema, an influence rarely acknowledged but no less impactful.<sup about="#mwt582" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Sandoval_15-2" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Sandoval"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Sandoval-15" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 14;" id="mwAwk"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAwo">[14]</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
        
        <p id="mwAws">The <i id="mwAww">Chicago Tribune</i> interviewed Davy Jones, who said, "We touched a lot of musicians, you know. I can't tell you the amount of people that have come up and said, 'I wouldn't have been a musician if it hadn't been for the Monkees.' It baffles me even now".<sup about="#mwt587" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-123" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-123"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-123" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 122;" id="mwAw0"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAw4">[122]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAw8">The Monkees found unlikely fans among musicians of the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Punk_rock" title="Punk rock" id="mwAxA">punk rock</a> period of the mid-1970s. Many of these punk performers had grown up on TV reruns of the series, and sympathized with the anti-industry, anti-establishment trend of their career. <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Sex_Pistols" title="Sex Pistols" id="mwAxE">Sex Pistols</a> and <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Minor_Threat" title="Minor Threat" id="mwAxI">Minor Threat</a> both recorded versions of "(I'm Not Your) Steppin' Stone" and it was often played live by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Toy_Love" title="Toy Love" id="mwAxM">Toy Love</a>. Japanese new wave pop group <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Plastics_(band)" title="Plastics (band)" id="mwAxQ">the Plastics</a> recorded a synthesizer and drum-machine version of "Last Train to Clarksville" for their 1979 album <i id="mwAxU">Welcome Back</i>.</p>
        
        <p id="mwAxY"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Glenn_A._Baker" title="Glenn A. Baker" id="mwAxc">Glenn A. Baker</a>, author of <i id="mwAxg">Monkeemania: The True Story of the Monkees</i>, described the Monkees as "rock's first great embarrassment" in 1986:</p>
        
        <blockquote id="mwAxk"><p id="mwAxo">Like an illegitimate child in a respectable family, the Monkees are destined to be regarded forever as rock's first great embarrassment; misunderstood and maligned like a mongrel at a ritzy dog show, or a test tube baby at the <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Holy_See" title="Holy See" id="mwAxs">Vatican</a>... The fact was ignored that session players were being heavily employed by <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Beach_Boys" title="The Beach Boys" id="mwAxw">the Beach Boys</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Beatles" title="The Beatles" id="mwAx0">the Beatles</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Mamas_and_the_Papas" title="The Mamas and the Papas" class="mw-redirect" id="mwAx4">the Mamas and the Papas</a>, <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Byrds" title="The Byrds" id="mwAx8">the Byrds</a> and other titans of the age. However, what could <i id="mwAyA">not</i> be ignored, as rock disdained its pubescent past, was a group of middle-aged Hollywood businessmen had actually assembled their concept of a profitable rock group and foisted it upon the world. What mattered was that the Monkees had success handed to them on a silver plate.<sup about="#mwt589" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-baker_32-3" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"baker"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-baker-32" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 31;" id="mwAyE"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAyI">[31]</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
        
        <p id="mwAyM"><i id="mwAyQ"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Mediaite" title="Mediaite" id="mwAyU">Mediaite</a></i> columnist <a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Paul_Levinson" title="Paul Levinson" id="mwAyY">Paul Levinson</a> noted that "The Monkees were the first example of something created in a medium—in this case, a rock group on television—that jumped off the screen to have big impact in the real world."<sup about="#mwt594" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Levinson_124-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Levinson"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Levinson-124"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Levinson-124" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 123;" id="mwAyc"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAyg">[123]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwAyk">When commenting on the death of Jones on February 29, 2012, <i id="mwAyo"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)" id="mwAys">Time</a></i> magazine contributor James Poniewozik praised the television show, saying that</p>
        
        <blockquote id="mwAyw"><div class="poem" typeof="mw:Extension/poem" about="#mwt601" data-mw='{"name":"poem","attrs":{},"body":{"extsrc":"... even if the show never meant to be more than entertainment and a hit-single generator, we shouldn&apos;t sell &apos;&apos;The Monkees&apos;&apos; short. It was far better TV than it had to be; during an era of formulaic domestic sitcoms and wacky comedies, it was a stylistically ambitious show, with a distinctive visual style, absurdist sense of humor and unusual story structure. Whatever Jones and the Monkees were meant to be, they became creative artists in their own right, and Jones&apos; chipper Brit-pop presence was a big reason they were able to produce work that was commercial, wholesome and yet impressively weird.&lt;ref name=\"Poniewozik\">{{cite magazine |last=Poniewozik |first=James |date=February 2012 |title=RIP Davy Jones, The Monkees&apos; Daydreamboat |url=http://entertainment.time.com/2012/02/29/rip-davy-jones-the-monkees-daydreamboat/?xid=rss-topstories |url-status=live |magazine=[[Time (magazine)|Time]] |archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130525005037/http://entertainment.time.com/2012/02/29/rip-davy-jones-the-monkees-daydreamboat/?xid=rss-topstories |archive-date=May 25, 2013 |access-date=February 29, 2012}}&lt;/ref>"}}' id="mwAy0"><p id="mwAy4">... even if the show never meant to be more than entertainment and a hit-single generator, we shouldn't sell <i id="mwAy8">The Monkees</i> short. It was far better TV than it had to be; during an era of formulaic domestic sitcoms and wacky comedies, it was a stylistically ambitious show, with a distinctive visual style, absurdist sense of humor and unusual story structure. Whatever Jones and the Monkees were meant to be, they became creative artists in their own right, and Jones' chipper Brit-pop presence was a big reason they were able to produce work that was commercial, wholesome and yet impressively weird.<sup about="#mwt600" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-Poniewozik_125-0" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{"name":"Poniewozik"},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-Poniewozik-125"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-Poniewozik-125" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 124;" id="mwAzA"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzE">[124]</span></a></sup></p></div></blockquote>
        
        <p id="mwAzI">The band released four chart-topping albums and three chart-topping songs ("Last Train to Clarksville", "I'm a Believer", and "Daydream Believer"), and sold more than 75 million records worldwide.<sup about="#mwt607" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-126" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-126"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-126" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 125;" id="mwAzM"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzQ">[125]</span></a></sup><sup about="#mwt612" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-127" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-127"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-127" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 126;" id="mwAzU"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzY">[126]</span></a></sup></p>
        <!--Both the style and substance of the Monkees were imitated by American boy band [[Big Time Rush]] (BTR), who performed in their own television series which—by admission of series creator Scott Fellows—was heavily influenced by the Monkees. Similarly to the Monkees, Big Time Rush featured a "made&#x2D;for&#x2D;tv" boy band often caught in a series of misadventures, hijinks, and somewhat slapstick comedy. The show, now in reruns but still hugely popular on [[Teen Nick]], is highly stylized and patterned after the Monkees, even capped with similar cartoonish sound effects. Like the Monkees, BTR has also seen critical and commercial success in America and worldwide through album, singles and high TV ratings worldwide.-->
        
        <section data-mw-section-id="29" id="mwAzc"><h3 id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</h3>
        <p id="mwAzg"><a rel="mw:WikiLink" href="./The_Criterion_Collection" title="The Criterion Collection" id="mwAzk">The Criterion Collection</a>, which has a stated goal to release "a continuing series of important classic and contemporary films, [and] has been dedicated to gathering the greatest films from around the world and publishing them in editions that offer the highest technical quality and award-winning, original supplements"<sup about="#mwt619" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-128" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-128"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-128" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 127;" id="mwAzo"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAzs">[127]</span></a></sup> recognized the Monkees' film <i id="mwAzw">Head</i> as meeting their criteria when they fully restored and released it on DVD and Blu-ray in 2010. They stated that <i id="mwAz0">Head</i> was "way, way ahead of its time" and "arguably the most authentically psychedelic film made in 1960s Hollywood".<sup about="#mwt623" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-129" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-129"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-129" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 128;" id="mwAz4"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwAz8">[128]</span></a></sup> <i id="mwA0A">Head</i> dodged commercial success on its release but has since been reclaimed as one of the great cult objects of its era."<sup about="#mwt627" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-130" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-130"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-130" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 129;" id="mwA0E"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA0I">[129]</span></a></sup></p>
        
        <p id="mwA0M">In the book <i id="mwA0Q">Hey, Hey We're The Monkees</i>, Rafelson wrote that, with <i id="mwA0U">Head</i>, he explored unprecedented cinematic techniques, including filming actors underwater, the use of polarization, and inventing "double-matte experiences". "When it was shown in France, the head of the Cinematheque overly praised the movie as a cinematic masterpiece, and from that point on, this movie began to acquire an underground reputation."<sup about="#mwt632" class="mw-ref reference" id="cite_ref-131" rel="dc:references" typeof="mw:Extension/ref" data-mw='{"name":"ref","attrs":{},"body":{"id":"mw-reference-text-cite_note-131"}}'><a href="./The_Monkees#cite_note-131" style="counter-reset: mw-Ref 130;" id="mwA0Y"><span class="mw-reflink-text" id="mwA0c">[130]</span></a></sup>
